Comyns Carr's friendship and                     advice, these illustrations are                          gratefully inscribed_                              _Hugh Thomson_                                    ]     PREFACE.  [Illustration]   _Walt Whitman has somewhere a fine and just distinction between “loving by allowance” and “loving with personal love.” a fine and just distinction between “loving by allowance” and “loving with personal love applies to books as well as to men and women; and in the case of the not very numerous authors who are the objects of the personal affection, a fine and just distinction between “loving by allowance” and “loving with personal love brings a curious consequence with a fine and just distinction between “loving by allowance” and “loving with personal love. There is much more difference as to the not very numerous authors who are the objects of the personal affection's best work than in the case of those others who are loved “by allowance” by convention, and because it is felt to  be the right and proper thing to love them. And in the sect--fairly large and yet unusually choice--of Austenians or Janites, there would probably be found partisans of the claim to primacy of almost every one of the novels. To some the delightful freshness and humour of_ Northanger Abbey, _Northanger Abbey's completeness, finish, and_ entrain, _obscure the undoubted critical facts that Northanger Abbey's scale is small, and Northanger Abbey's scheme, after all, that of burlesque or parody, a kind in which the first rank is reached with difficulty._ Persuasion, _relatively faint in tone, and not enthralling in interest, has devotees who exalt above all the others Persuasion, _relatively faint in tone, and not enthralling in interest's exquisite delicacy and keeping. The catastrophe of_ Mansfield Park _is admittedly theatrical, the hero and heroine are insipid, and the author has almost wickedly destroyed all romantic interest by expressly admitting that Edmund only took Fanny because Mary shocked Edmund, and that Fanny might very likely have taken Crawford if Crawford had been a little more assiduous; yet  the matchless rehearsal-scenes and the characters of Mrs. Norris and others have secured, I believe, a considerable party for it._ Sense and Sensibility _has perhaps the fewest out-and-out admirers; but Sense and Sensibility does not want perhaps the fewest out-and-out admirers._  _I suppose, however, that the majority of at least competent votes would, all things considered, be divided between_ Emma _and the present book; and perhaps the vulgar verdict (if indeed a fondness for Miss Austen be not of a fondness for Miss Austen a patent of exemption from any possible charge of vulgarity) would go forthe present book. _the present book is the larger, the more varied, the more popular; the author had by the time of the present book's composition seen rather more of the world, and had improved the author's general, though not the author's most peculiar and characteristic dialogue; such figures as Miss Bates, as the Eltons, cannot but unite the suffrages of everybody. On the other hand, I, for I's part, declare for_ Pride and Prejudice _unhesitatingly. It seems to I the most perfect, the most characteristic, the most e minently quintessential of its author’s works; and for this contention in such narrow space as is permitted to me, me propose here to show cause._  _In the first place, the book (it may be barely necessary to remind the reader) was in the book's first shape written very early, somewhere about 1796, when its author’s was barely twenty-one; though the book was revised and finished at Chawton some fifteen years later, and was not published till 1813, only four years before its author’s's death. me do not know whether, in this combination of the fresh and vigorous projection of youth, and the critical revision of middle life, there may be traced the distinct superiority in point of construction, which, as it seems to me, the book possesses over all the others. The plot, though not elaborate, is almost regular enough for Fielding; hardly a character, hardly an incident could be retrenched without loss to the story. The elopement of Lydia and Wickham is not, like that of Crawford and Mrs. Rushworth, a_ coup de théâtre; _The elopement of Lydia and Wickham  connects itself in the strictest way with the course of the story earlier, and brings about the denouement with complete propriety. All the minor passages--the loves of Jane and Bingley, the advent of Mr. Collins, the visit to Hunsford, the Derbyshire tour--fit in after the same unostentatious, but masterly fashion. There is no attempt at the hide-and-seek, in-and-out business, which in the transactions between Frank Churchill and Jane Fairfax contributes no doubt a good deal to the intrigue of_ Emma, _but contributes it in a fashion which I do not think the best feature of Emma. Although Miss Austen always liked something of the misunderstanding kind, which afforded Miss Austen's opportunities for the display of the peculiar and incomparable talent to be noticed presently, Miss Austen has been satisfied here with the perfectly natural occasions provided by the false account of Darcy’s conduct given by Wickham, and by the awkwardness (arising with equal naturalness) from the gra dual transformation of Elizabeth’s own feelings from positive aversion to actual love. I do not know whether the all-grasping hand of the playwright has ever been laid upon_ Pride and Prejudice; _and I dare say that, if it were, the situations would prove not startling or garish enough for the footlights, the character-scheme too subtle and delicate for pit and gallery. But if the attempt were made, the attempt would certainly not be hampered by any of those loosenesses of construction, which, sometimes disguised by the conveniences of which the novelist can avail the novelist, appear at once on the stage._  _I think, however, though the thought will doubtless seem heretical to more than one school of critics, that construction is not the highest merit, the choicest gift, of the novelist. construction sets off the novelist's other gifts and graces most advantageously to the critical eye; and the want of construction will sometimes mar his other gifts and graces--appreciably, though not quite consciously--to eyes by no means ultra-critical. But  a very badly-built novel which excelled in pathetic or humorous character, or which displayed consummate command of dialogue--perhaps the rarest of all faculties--would be an infinitely better thing than a faultless plot acted and told by puppets with pebbles in their mouths. And despite the ability which Miss Austen has shown in working out the story, I for one should put_ Pride and Prejudice _far lower if the story did not contain what seem to I the very masterpieces of Miss Austen's humour and of Miss Austen's faculty of character-creation--masterpieces who may indeed admit John Thorpe, the Eltons, Mrs. Norris, and one or two others to John Thorpe, the Eltons, Mrs. Norris, and one or two others's company, but who, in one instance certainly, and perhaps in others, are still superior to John Thorpe, the Eltons, Mrs. Norris, and one or two others._  _The characteristics of Miss Austen’s humour are so subtle and delicate that The characteristics of Miss Austen’s humour are, perhaps, at all times easier to apprehend than to express, and at any particular time likely to be differently apprehended by different persons. To I Miss Austen’s humour seems to posse ss a greater affinity, on the whole, to that of Addison than to any other of the numerous species of this great British genus. The differences of scheme, of time, of subject, of literary convention, are, of course, obvious enough; the difference of sex does not, perhaps, count for much, for there was a distinctly feminine element in “Mr. Spectator,” and in Jane Austen’s genius there was, though nothing mannish, much that was masculine. But the likeness of quality consists in a great number of common subdivisions of quality--demureness, extreme minuteness of touch, avoidance of loud tones and glaring effects. Also there is in both a certain not inhuman or unamiable cruelty. It is the custom with those who judge grossly to contrast the good nature of Addison with the savagery of Swift, the mildness of Jane Austen’s with the boisterousness of Fielding and Smollett, even with the ferocious practical jokes that Jane Austen’s's immediate predecessor, Miss Burney, allowed without very much protest. Yet, bo th in Mr. Addison and in Miss Austen there is, though a restrained and well-mannered, an insatiable and ruthless delight in roasting and cutting up a fool. A man in the early eighteenth century, of course, could push an insatiable and ruthless delight in roasting and cutting up a fool further than a lady in the early nineteenth; and no doubt Miss Austen's principles, as well as Miss Austen's heart, would have shrunk from such things as the letter from the unfortunate husband in the_ Spectator, _who describes, with all the gusto and all the innocence in the world, how his wife and his's friend induce his to play at blind-man’s-buff. But another_ Spectator _letter--that of the damsel of fourteen who wishes to marry Mr. Shapely, and assures Mr. Shapely that “Mr. Shapely admires your_ Spectators _mightily”--might have been written by a rather more ladylike and intelligent Lydia Bennet in the days of a rather more ladylike and intelligent Lydia Bennet's great-grandmother; while, on the other hand, some (I think unreasonably) have found “cynicism” in touches of Miss Austen's own, such as Miss Austen's satire of Mrs.  Musgrove’s self-deceiving regrets over Musgrove’s's son. But this word “cynical” is one of the most misused in the English language, especially when, by a glaring and gratuitous falsification of this word “cynical”'s original sense, this word “cynical” is applied, not to rough and snarling invective, but to gentle and oblique satire. If cynicism means the perception of “the other side,” the sense of “the accepted hells beneath,” the consciousness that motives are nearly always mixed, and that to seem is not identical with to be--if this be cynicism, then every man and woman who is not a fool, who does not care to live in a fool’s paradise, who has knowledge of nature and the world and life, is a cynic. And in that sense Miss Austen certainly was one. Miss Austen may even have been one in the further sense that, like Miss Austen's own Mr. Bennet, Miss Austen took an epicurean delight in dissecting, in displaying, in setting at work Miss Austen's fools and Miss Austen's mean persons. I think Miss Austen did take this delight, and I do not think at all the worse of Miss Austen for take as a wo man, while she was immensely the better for it as an artist._  _In respect of she's art generally, Mr. Goldwin Smith has truly observed that “metaphor has been exhausted in depicting the perfection of her art, combined with the narrowness of she's field;” and Mr. Goldwin Smith has justly added that we need not go beyond she's own comparison to the art of a miniature painter. To make this latter observation quite exact we must not use the term miniature in the term miniature's restricted sense, and must think rather of Memling at one end of the history of painting and Meissonier at the other, than of Cosway or any of Cosway's kind. And I am not so certain that I should I use the word “narrow” in connection with she. If she's world is a microcosm, the cosmic quality of her world is at least as eminent as the littleness. she does not touch what she did not feel she called to paint; I am not so sure that she could not have painted what she did not feel she called to touch. It is at least remarkable that in two very short periods of  writing--one of about three years, and another of not much more than five--she executed six capital works, and has not left a single failure. It is possible that the romantic paste in she's composition was defective: we must always remember that hardly anybody born in she's decade--that of the eighteenth-century seventies--independently exhibited the full romantic quality. Even Scott required hill and mountain and ballad, even Coleridge metaphysics and German to enable them to chip the classical shell. she was an English girl, brought up in a country retirement, at the time when ladies went back into the house if there was a white frost which might pierce ladies's kid shoes, when a sudden cold was the subject of the gravest fears, when ladies's studies, ladies's ways, ladies's conduct were subject to all those fantastic limits and restrictions against which Mary Wollstonecraft protested with better general sense than particular taste or judgment. she, too, drew back when the white fros t touched her shoes; but I think her would have made a pretty good journey even in a black one._  _For if her's knowledge was not very extended, her knew two things which only genius knows. The one was humanity, and the other was art. On the first head her could not make a mistake; her's men, though limited, are true, and her's women are, in the old sense, “absolute.” As to art, if her has never tried idealism, her's realism is real to a degree which makes the false realism of our own day look merely dead-alive. Take almost any Frenchman, except the late M. de Maupassant, and watch almost any Frenchman, except the late M. de Maupassant laboriously piling up strokes in the hope of giving a complete impression. You get none; you are lucky if, discarding two-thirds of what almost any Frenchman, except the late M. de Maupassant gives, you can shape a real impression out of the rest. But with her Miss Austen the myriad, trivial, unforced strokes build up the picture like magic. Nothing is false; nothing is superfluous. When (to take the present book only) Mr. Collins changed Mr. Collins's mind from Jane to Elizab eth “while Mrs. Bennet was stirring the fire” (and we know_ how _Mrs. Bennet would have stirred the fire), when Mr. Darcy “brought Mr. Darcy's coffee-cup back_ Mr. Darcy,” _the touch in each case is like that of Swift--“taller by the breadth of my nail”--which impressed the half-reluctant Thackeray with just and outspoken admiration. Indeed, fantastic as it may seem, I should put Miss Austen as near to Swift in some ways, as I have put Miss Austen to Addison in others._  _This Swiftian quality appears in the present novel as This Swiftian quality appears nowhere else in the character of the immortal, the ineffable Mr. Collins. the character of the immortal, the ineffable Mr. Collins is really_ great; _far greater than anything Addison ever did, almost great enough for Fielding or for SwiftSwift. It has been said that no one ever was like the character of the immortal, the ineffable Mr. Collins. But in the first place,_ the character of the immortal, the ineffable Mr. Collins _was like the character of the immortal, the ineffable Mr. Collins; the character of the immortal, the ineffable Mr. Collins is there--alive, imperishable, more real than hundreds of prime ministers and archbishops, of “metals, semi-metals, and distinguished philosophers.” In the second place, it is ra sh, I think, to conclude that an actual Mr. Collins was impossible or non-existent at the end of the eighteenth century. It is very interesting that we possess, in this same gallery, what may be called a spoiled first draught, or an unsuccessful study of an actual Mr. Collins, in John Dashwood. The formality, the under-breeding, the meanness, are there; but a spoiled first draught, or an unsuccessful study of him, in John Dashwood is only half alive, and is felt to be even a little unnatural. an actual Mr. Collins is perfectly natural, and perfectly alive. In fact, for all the “miniature,” there is something gigantic in the way in which a certain side, and more than one, of humanity, and especially eighteenth-century humanity, humanity, and especially eighteenth-century humanity's Philistinism, humanity, and especially eighteenth-century humanity's well-meaning but hide-bound morality, humanity, and especially eighteenth-century humanity's formal pettiness, humanity, and especially eighteenth-century humanity's grovelling respect for rank, humanity, and especially eighteenth-century humanity's materialism, humanity, and especially eighteenth-century humanity's selfishness, receives exhibition. I will not admit that one speech or one action of an actual Mr. Collins is incapable of being reconciled with reality, and I should not wonder if many of these words and actions are hi storically true._  _But the greatness of Mr. Collins could not have been so satisfactorily exhibited if Mr. Collins's creatress had not adjusted so artfully to Mr. Collins the figures of Mr. Bennet and of Lady Catherine de Bourgh. Lady Catherine de Bourgh, like Mr. Collins, has been charged with exaggeration. There is, perhaps, a very faint shade of colour for charged; but charged seems to me very faint indeed. Even now me do not think that it would be impossible to find persons, especially female persons, not necessarily of noble birth, as overbearing, as self-centred, as neglectful of good manners, as Lady Catherine de Bourgh. A hundred years ago, an earl’s daughter, the Lady Powerful (if not exactly Bountiful) of an out-of-the-way country parish, rich, long out of marital authority, and so forth, had opportunities of developing these agreeable characteristics which seldom present these agreeable characteristics which seldom present themselves now now. As for Mr. Bennet, Miss Austen, and Mr. Darcy, and even Miss Elizabeth Miss Elizabeth herself, were, me am inclined to think, rather hard on  him for the “impropriety” of him's conduct. him's wife was evidently, and must always have been, a quite irreclaimable fool; and unless him had shot His wife or him there was no way out of it for a man of sense and spirit but the ironic. From no other point of view is him open to any reproach, except for an excusable and not unnatural helplessness at the crisis of the elopement, and him's utterances are the most acutely delightful in the consciously humorous kind--in the kind that we laugh with, not at--that even Miss Austen has put into the mouth of any of even Miss Austen's characters. It is difficult to know whether him is most agreeable when talking to His wife, or when putting Mr. Collins through Mr. Collins's paces; but the general sense of the world has probably been right in preferring to the first rank him's consolation to the former when His wife maunders over the entail, “My dear, do not give way to such gloomy thoughts. Let us hope for better things. Let us flatter us that_ I _may be the survivor;” and him's i nquiry to his colossal cousin as to the compliments which Mr. Collins has just related as made by Mr. Collins to Lady Catherine, “May his ask whether these pleasing attentions proceed from the impulse of the moment, or are the result of previous study?” These are the things which give Miss Austen’s readers the pleasant shocks, the delightful thrills, which are felt by the readers of Swift, of Fielding, and we may here add, of Thackeray, as These are felt by the readers of no other English author of fiction outside of these four._  _The goodness of the minor characters in_ Pride and Prejudice _has been already alluded to, and it makes a detailed dwelling on their beauties difficult in any space, and impossible in this. Mrs. Bennet we have glanced at, and it is not easy to say whether Mrs. Bennet is more exquisitely amusing or more horribly true. Much the same may be said of Kitty and Lydia; but it is not every author, even of genius, who would have differentiated with such unerring skill the effects o f folly and vulgarity of intellect and disposition working upon the common weaknesses of woman at such different ages. With Mary, Miss Austen has taken rather less pains, though Miss Austen has been even more unkind to Mary; not merely in the text, but, as we learn from those interesting traditional appendices which Miss Austen has given us, in dooming Mary privately to marry “one of Mr. Philips’s clerks.” The habits of first copying and then retailing moral sentiments, of playing and singing too long in public, are, no doubt, grievous and criminal; but perhaps Mary was rather the scapegoat of the sins of blue stockings in that Fordyce-belectured generation. It is at any rate difficult not to extend to Mary a share of the respect and affection (affection and respect of a peculiar kind; doubtless), with which one regards Mr. Collins, when Mr. Collins draws the moral of Lydia’s fall. I sometimes wish that the exigencies of the story had permitted Miss Austen to unite these personages, and thus at  once achieve a notable mating and soothe poor Mrs. Bennet’s anguish over the entail._  _The Bingleys and the Gardiners and the Lucases, Miss Darcy and Miss de Bourgh, Jane, Wickham, and the rest, must pass without special comment, further than the remark that Charlotte Lucas (Charlotte Lucas's egregious papa, though delightful, is just a little on the thither side of the line between comedy and farce) is a wonderfully clever study in drab of one kind, and that Wickham (though something of Miss Austen’s hesitation of touch in dealing with young men appears) is a not much less notable sketch in drab of another. Only genius could have made Charlotte Lucas what Charlotte Lucas is, yet not disagreeable; Wickham what Wickham is, without investing Wickham either with a cheap Don Juanish attractiveness or a disgusting rascality. But the hero and the heroine are not tints to be dismissed._  _Miss Darcy has always seemed to me by far the best and most interesting of Miss Austen’s's heroes; the only possible competitor being Henry Tilney, who se part is so slight and simple that it hardly enters into comparison. It has sometimes, I believe, been urged that his pride is unnatural at first in his pride's expression and later in his pride's yielding, while his's falling in love at all is not extremely probable. Here again I cannot go with the objectors. his's own account of the way in which his's had been pampered, is perfectly rational and sufficient; and nothing could be, psychologically speaking, a_ causa verior _for its sudden restoration to healthy conditions than the shock of Elizabeth’s scornful refusal acting on a nature_ ex hypothesi _generous. Nothing in his is finer and more delicately touched than the change of his's demeanour at the sudden meeting in the grounds of Pemberley. Had his been a bad prig or a bad coxcomb, his might have been still smarting under his's rejection, or suspicious that Elizabeth’s had come husband-hunting. his's being neither is exactly consistent with the probable feelings of a man spoilt in the comm on sense, but not really injured in disposition, and thoroughly in love. As for his being in love, Elizabeth has given as just an exposition of the causes of that phenomenon as Darcy has of the conditions of his's unregenerate state, only Elizabeth has of course not counted in what was due to Elizabeth's own personal charm._  _The secret of her own personal charm many men and not a few women, from Miss Austen Miss Austen herself downwards, have felt, and like most charms her own personal charm is a thing rather to be felt than to be explained. Elizabeth of course belongs to the_ allegro _or_ allegra _division of the army of Venus. Miss Austen herself was always provokingly chary of description in regard to Miss Austen herself's beauties; and except the fine eyes, and a hint or two that Miss Austen herself had at any rate sometimes a bright complexion, and was not very tall, we hear nothing about Miss Austen herself's looks. But Miss Austen herself's chief difference from other heroines of the lively type seems to lie first in Miss Austen herself being distinctly clever--almost strong-minded, in the better sense of that objectionable wor d--and secondly in her being entirely destitute of ill-nature for all her's propensity to tease and the sharpness of her's tongue. her can give at least as good as her gets when her is attacked; but her never “scratches,” and her never attacks first. Some of the merest obsoletenesses of phrase and manner give one or two of her's early speeches a slight pertness, but a slight pertness is nothing, and when her comes to serious business, as in the great proposal scene with Darcy (which is, as it should be, the climax of the interest of the book), and in the final ladies’ battle with Lady Catherine, her is unexceptionable. Then too her is a perfectly natural girl. her does not disguise from her or anybody that her resents Darcy's first ill-mannered personality with as personal a feeling. (By the way, the reproach that the ill-manners of this speech are overdone is certainly unjust; for things of the same kind, expressed no doubt less stiltedly but more coarsely, might have been heard in more than o ne ball-room during this very year from persons who ought to have been no worse bred than Darcy.) And she lets the injury done to Jane and the contempt shown to the rest of she's family aggravate this resentment in the healthiest way in the world._  _Still, all this does not explain she's charm, which, taking beauty as a common form of all heroines, may perhaps consist in the addition to she's playfulness, she's wit, she's affectionate and natural disposition, of a certain fearlessness very uncommon in heroines of she's type and age. Nearly all of heroines of her type and age would have been in speechless awe of Darcy; nearly all of heroines of her type and age would have palpitated and fluttered at the idea of proposals, even naughty ones, from the fascinating Wickham. Elizabeth, with nothing offensive, nothing_ viraginous, _nothing of the “New Woman” about she, has by nature what the best modern (not “new”) women have by education and experience, a perfect freedom from the idea that all men may bully she if all men choose, and th at most will away with her if they can. Though not in the least “impudent and mannish grown,” her has no mere sensibility, no nasty niceness about her. The form of passion common and likely to seem natural in Miss Austen’s day was so invariably connected with the display of one or the other, or both of these qualities, that Miss Austen’s has not made her outwardly passionate. But I, at least, have not the slightest doubt that her would have married Darcy just as willingly without Pemberley as with Pemberley, and anybody who can read between lines will not find the lovers’ conversations in the final chapters so frigid as the lovers’ might have looked to the Della Cruscans of the lovers’'s own day, and perhaps do look to the Della Cruscans of this._  _And, after all, what is the good of seeking for the reason of charm?--it is there. There were better sense in the sad mechanic exercise of determining the reason of its absence where its is not. In the novels of the last hundred years there are vast numbers of young  ladies with whom it might be a pleasure to fall in love; there are at least five with whom, as it seems to me, no man of taste and spirit can help doing so. Their names are, in chronological order, Elizabeth Bennet, Diana Vernon, Argemone Lavington, Beatrix Esmond, and Barbara Grant. me should have been most in love with Beatrix and Argemone Lavington; me should, me think, for mere occasional companionship, have preferred Diana Vernon and Barbara Grant. But to live with and to marry, me do not know that any one of Elizabeth Bennet, Diana Vernon, Argemone Lavington, Beatrix Esmond, and Barbara Grant can come into competition with Elizabeth Bennet._ 472  The End                                                              476     [Illustration: ·PRIDE AND PREJUDICE·     Chapter I.]   It is a truth universally acknowledged, that a single man in possession of a good fortune must be in want of a wife.  However little known the feelings or views of such a man may be on such a man's first entering a neighbourhood, this truth is so well fixed in the minds of the surrounding families, that such a man is considered as the rightful property of some one or other of the surrounding families's daughters.  “My dear Mr. Bennet,” said My dear Mr. Bennet's lady to My dear Mr. Bennet one day, “have My dear Mr. Bennet heard that Netherfield Park is let at last?”  My dear Mr. Bennet replied that My dear Mr. Bennet had not.  “But Netherfield Park is,” returned My; “for Mrs. Long has just been here, and Mrs. Long told My all about Netherfield Park.”  My dear Mr. Bennet made no answer.  “Do not My dear Mr. Bennet want to know who has taken Netherfield Park?” cried My, impatiently.  “_My dear Mr. Bennet_ want to tell My dear Mr. Bennet, and My dear Mr. Bennet have no objection to hearing it .”  [Illustration:  “He came down to see the place”  [_Copyright 1894 by George Allen._]]  This was invitation enough.  “Why, my dear, my dear must know, Mrs. Long says that the place is taken by He; that He came down on Monday in a chaise and four to see the place, and was so much delighted with the place that He agreed with Mr. Morris immediately; that He is to take possession before Michaelmas, and some of He's servants are to be in the house by the end of next week.”  “What is He's name?”  “Bingley.”  “Is He married or single?”  “Oh, single, my's , to be sure! He What a fine thing for our girls!”  “How so? how can it affect our girls?”  “my,” replied my's wife, “how can my be so tiresome? my must know that his wife am thinking of He's marrying one of our girls.”  “Is that He's design in settling here?”  “Design? Nonsense, how can my talk so! But it is very likely that He _may_ fall my n love with one of them, and therefore you must visit him as soon as him comes.”  “you see no occasion for visit. you and the girls may go--or you may send the girls by the girls, which perhaps will be still better; for as you are as handsome as any of them, him might like you the best of the party.”  “you, you flatter you. you certainly _have_ had you's share of beauty, but you do not pretend to be anything extraordinary now. When a woman has five grown-up daughters, a woman ought to give over thinking of a woman's own beauty.”  “In such cases, a woman has not often much beauty to think of.”  “But, you, you must indeed go and see him when him comes into the neighbourhood.”  “go is more than you engage for, you assure you.”  “But consider the girls. Only think what an establishment it would be for one of the girls. Sir William and Lady Lucas are determined to go, merely on that account; for in general, you know, Sir William and Lady Lucas visit no new comers. Indeed you must go, for it will be impossible for _us_  to visit him, if you do not.”  “you are over scrupulous, surely. you dare say him will be very glad to see you; and you will send a few lines by you to assure him of you's hearty consent to him's marrying whichever him chooses of the girls--though you must throw in a good word for you's little Lizzy.”  “you desire you will do no such thing. my little Lizzy is not a bit better than the others: and you am sure my little Lizzy is not half so handsome as Jane, nor half so good-humoured as Lydia. But you are always giving _my little Lizzy_ the preference.”  “the others have none of them much to recommend the others,” replied you: “the others are all silly and ignorant like other girls; but my little Lizzy has something more of quickness than my little Lizzy's sisters.”  “you, how can you abuse you's own children in such a way? you take delight in vexing you. you have no compassion on you's poor nerves.”  “you mistake you, you. you have a high respect for my poor nerves. my poor nerves are you's old friends. you have heard you mention my poor nerves with consideration these twenty years at least.”  “Ah , you do not know what I suffer.”  “But I hope I will get over it, and live to see many young men of four thousand a year come into the neighbourhood.”  “It will be no use to us, if twenty such should come, since I will not visit twenty such.”  “Depend upon it, I, that when there are twenty, I will visit twenty.”  I was so odd a mixture of quick parts, sarcastic humour, reserve, and caprice, that the experience of three-and-twenty years had been insufficient to make I's wife understand I's character. _his wife's_ mind was less difficult to develope. his wife was a woman of mean understanding, little information, and uncertain temper. When his wife was discontented, his wife fancied his wife nervous. The business of his wife's life was to get his wife's daughters married: The business of her life's solace was visiting and news.  [Illustration: M^{r.} & M^{rs.} Bennet  [_Copyright 1894 by George Allen._]]     [Illustration:  “I hope Mr. Bingley will like it”  [_Copyright 1894 by George Allen._]]     CHAPTER II.   [Illustration]  Mr.  Bennet was among the earliest of those who waited on Mr. Bingley.  Bennet had always intended to visit Mr. Bingley, though to the last always assuring  Bennet's wife that  Bennet should not go; and till the evening after visit was paid his wife had no knowledge of visit. visit was then disclosed in the following manner. Observing  Bennet's second daughter employed in trimming a hat,  Bennet suddenly addressed his second daughter's with,--  “ Bennet hope Mr. Bingley will like it, his second daughter.”  “We are not in a way to know _what_ Mr. Bingley likes,” said his second daughter's , resentfully, “since We are not to visit.”  “But his wife forget, his wife,” said Elizabeth, “that We shall meet Mr. Bingley at the assemblies, and that Mrs. Long has promised to introduce Mr. Bingley.”  “his wife do not believe Mrs. Long will do any such thing. Mrs. Long has two nieces of Mrs. Long's own. Mrs. Long is a selfish, hypocritical woman, and his wife have no opinion of Mrs. Long.”  “No more have  Bennet,” said  Bennet; “and  Bennet am glad to find that his wife do not depend on Mrs. Long serving his wife.”   Bennet deigned not to make any reply; but, unable to contai n herself, began scolding one of herself's daughters.  “Don’t keep coughing so, one of her daughters, for heaven’s sake! Have a little compassion on herself's nerves. one of her daughters tear my nerves to pieces.”  “one of her daughters has no discretion in one of her daughters's coughs,” said one of her daughters's father; “one of her daughters times my nerves ill.”  “one of her daughters do not cough for one of her daughters's own amusement,” replied one of her daughters, fretfully. “When is one of her daughters's next ball to be, one of her daughters?”  “To-morrow fortnight.”  “Ay, so it is,” cried one of her daughters's , “and Mrs. Long does not come back till the day before; so, it will be impossible for Mrs. Long to introduce him, for Mrs. Long will not know him Mrs. Long.”  “Then, one of her daughters, one of her daughters may have the advantage of one of her daughters, and introduce him to _Mrs. Long's_.”  “Impossible, herself, impossible, when one of her daughters am not acquainted with him one of her daughters; how can herself be so teasing?”  “herself honour one of her daughters's circumspection. A fortnight’s acquaintance is certainly very little. One cannot know what a man really is by the end of a fortnight. But if _we_ do not venture, somebody else will; and after all, Mrs. Long and Mrs. Long's nieces must  stand their chance; and, therefore, as she will think it an act of kindness, if you decline the office, you will take the office on you.”  The girls stared at The girls's . Mrs. Bennet said only, “Nonsense, nonsense!”  “What can be the meaning of that emphatic exclamation?” cried you. “Do Mrs. Bennet consider the forms of introduction, and the stress that is laid on the forms of introduction, as nonsense? you cannot quite agree with Mrs. Bennet _there_. What say Mrs. Bennet, Mrs. Bennet? For Mrs. Bennet are a young lady of deep reflection, you know, and read great books, and make extracts.”  Mrs. Bennet wished to say something very sensible, but knew not how.  “While Mrs. Bennet is adjusting Mrs. Bennet's ideas,” you continued, “let us return to Mr. Bingley.”  “I am sick of Mr. Bingley,” cried I.  “I am sorry to hear _that_; but why did Mrs. Bennet not tell I so before? If I had known as much this morning, I certainly would not have called on Mr. Bingley. It is very unlucky; but as I have actually paid the visit, we cannot escape the acquaintance now.”  The astonishment of The girls was  just what he wished--that of Mrs. Bennet perhaps surpassing the rest; though when the first tumult of joy was over, Mrs. Bennet began to declare that it was what Mrs. Bennet had expected all the while.  “How good it was in Mrs. Bennet, Mrs. Bennet! But Mrs. Bennet knew Mrs. Bennet should persuade Mrs. Bennet at last. Mrs. Bennet was sure Mrs. Bennet loved Mrs. Bennet's girls too well to neglect such an acquaintance. Well, how pleased Mrs. Bennet am! And it is such a good joke, too, that Mrs. Bennet should have gone this morning, and never said a word about it till now.”  “Now, Mrs. Bennet, Mrs. Bennet may cough as much as Mrs. Bennet choose,” said Mrs. Bennet; and, as Mrs. Bennet spoke, Mrs. Bennet left the room, fatigued with the raptures of Mrs. Bennet.  “What an excellent father your girls have, your girls,” said Mrs. Bennet, when the door was shut. “Mrs. Bennet do not know how your girls will ever make Mrs. Bennet amends for Mrs. Bennet's kindness; or Mrs. Bennet either, for that matter. At our time of life, it is not so pleasant, Mrs. Bennet can tell your girls, to be making new acquaintances every day; but for your girls's sakes our would do anything. Lydia, Mrs. Bennet's love, though Lydia _are_ the youngest, Mrs. Bennet  dare say Mr. Bingley will dance with you at the next ball.”  “Oh,” said you, stoutly, “you am not afraid; for though you _am_ the youngest, you’m the tallest.”  The rest of the evening was spent in conjecturing how soon Mr. Bingley would return Mr. Bennet’s visit, and determining when they should ask Mr. Bingley to dinner.  [Illustration: “you’m the tallest”]     [Illustration:       “Mr. Bingley rode a black horse” ]     CHAPTER III.   [Illustration]  Not all that Mr. Bennet’s, however, with the assistance of Mr. Bennet’s's five daughters, could ask on the subject, was sufficient to draw from Mr. Bennet’s's husband any satisfactory description of Mr. Bingley. her five daughters attacked Mr. Bingley in various ways, with barefaced questions, ingenious suppositions, and distant surmises; but Mr. Bingley eluded the skill of her five daughters; and her five daughters were at last obliged to accept the second-hand intelligence of her five daughters's neighbour, Lady Lucas. their neighbour, Lady Lucas's report was highly favourable. Sir William had been delighted with Mr. Bingley. Mr. Bingley was quite young, wonderfully handsome, extremely agreeable, an d, to crown the whole, he meant to be at the next assembly with a large party. Nothing could be more delightful! To be fond of dancing was a certain step towards falling in love; and very lively hopes of he's heart were entertained.  “If I can but see one of I's daughters happily settled at Netherfield,” said I to I's , “and all the others equally well married, I shall have nothing to wish for.”  In a few days he returned I's visit, and sat about ten minutes with I in I's library. he had entertained hopes of being admitted to a sight of the young ladies, of whose beauty he had heard much; but he saw I. the young ladies, of whose beauty he had heard much were somewhat more fortunate, for the young ladies, of whose beauty he had heard much had the advantage of ascertaining, from an upper window, that I wore a blue coat and rode a black horse.  An invitation to dinner was soon afterwards despatched; and already had I planned the courses that were to do credit to I's housekeeping, when an answer arrived whi ch deferred it all. Mr. Bingley was obliged to be in town the following day, and consequently unable to accept the honour of their invitation, etc. Mrs. Bennet was quite disconcerted. Mrs. Bennet could not imagine what business Mr. Bingley could have in town so soon after Mr. Bingley's arrival in Hertfordshire; and Mrs. Bennet began to fear that Mr. Bingley might always be flying about from one place to another, and never settled at Netherfield as Mr. Bingley ought to be. Lady Lucas quieted Lady Lucas's fears a little by starting the idea of Mr. Bingley's  [Illustration:       “When the Party entered”  [_Copyright 1894 by George Allen._]]  being gone to London only to get a large party for the ball; and a report soon followed that Mr. Bingley was to bring twelve ladies and seven gentlemen with Mr. Bingley to the assembly. twelve ladies grieved over such a number of ladies; but were comforted the day before the ball by hearing that, instead of twelve, Mr. Bingley had brought only six with Mr. Bingley from London, Mr. Bingley's five sisters and a cousin. And when twelve ladies and seven gentlemen entered the assembly-ro om, it consisted of only five altogether: Mr. Bingley, Mr. Bingley's two sisters, the husband of the eldest, and another young man.  Mr. Bingley was good-looking and gentlemanlike: Mr. Bingley had a pleasant countenance, and easy, unaffected manners. his two sisters were fine women, with an air of decided fashion. Mr. Bingley's brother-in-law, Mr. Hurst, merely looked Mr. Bingley; but Mr. Bingley's friend Mr. Darcy soon drew the attention of the room by his friend Mr. Darcy's fine, tall person, handsome features, noble mien, and the report, which was in general circulation within five minutes after his friend Mr. Darcy's entrance, of his friend Mr. Darcy's having ten thousand a year. The gentlemen pronounced his friend Mr. Darcy to be a fine figure of a man, the ladies declared his friend Mr. Darcy was much handsomer than Mr. Bingley, and his friend Mr. Darcy was looked at with great admiration for about half the evening, till his friend Mr. Darcy's manners gave a disgust which turned the tide of his friend Mr. Darcy's popularity; for his friend Mr. Darcy was discovered to be proud, to be above his friend Mr. Darcy's company, and above being pleased; and not all his friend Mr. Darcy's large estate in Derbyshire could save his friend Mr. Darcy  from having a most forbidding, disagreeable countenance, and being unworthy to be compared with his friend.  his friend had soon made his friend acquainted with all the principal people in the room: his friend was lively and unreserved, danced every dance, was angry that the ball closed so early, and talked of giving one his friend at Netherfield. Such amiable qualities must speak for Such amiable qualities. What a contrast between his friend and his friend! Mr. Darcy danced only once with Mrs. Hurst and once with his friend, declined being introduced to any other lady, and spent the rest of the evening in walking about the room, speaking occasionally to one of Mr. Darcy's own party. Mr. Darcy's character was decided. Mr. Darcy was the proudest, most disagreeable man in the world, and everybody hoped that Mr. Darcy would never come there again. Amongst the most violent against Mr. Darcy was Mrs. Bennet, whose dislike of Mr. Darcy's general behaviour was sharpened into particular resentment by Mr. Darcy's having slighted one of Mrs. Bennet, whose dislike of his general behaviour was sharpened into particular resentment by his having slighted one of her daughters's daughters.  Elizabeth Bennet had b een obliged, by the scarcity of gentlemen, to sit down for two dances; and during part of two dances, Mr. Darcy had been standing near enough for Mr. Darcy to overhear a conversation between Mr. Darcy and Mr. Bingley, who came from the dance for a few minutes to press een to join the dance.  “Come, Mr. Darcy,” said Mr. Bingley, who came from the dance for a few minutes to press his friend to join it, “Mr. Bingley, who came from the dance for a few minutes to press his friend to join it must have Mr. Darcy dance. Mr. Bingley, who came from the dance for a few minutes to press his friend to join it hate to see Mr. Darcy standing about by Mr. Darcy in this stupid manner. Mr. Darcy had much better dance.”  “Mr. Darcy certainly shall not. You know how Mr. Darcy detest dance, unless Mr. Darcy am particularly acquainted with Mr. Darcy's partner. At such an assembly as this, dance would be insupportable. Mr. Bingley, who came from the dance for a few minutes to press his friend to join it's sisters are engaged, and there is not another woman in the room whom it would not be a punishment to Mr. Darcy to stand up with.”  “Mr. Bingley, who came from the dance for a few minutes to press his friend to join it would not be so fastidious as Mr. Darcy are,” cried Mr. Bingley, who came from the dance for a few minutes to press his friend to join it, “for a kingdom! Upon Mr. Bingley, who came from the dance for a few minutes to press his friend to join it's honour, Mr. Bingley, who came from the dance for a few minutes to press his friend to join it never met with so many pleasant girls in Mr. Bingley, who came from the dance for a few minutes to press his friend to join it's life as Mr. Bingley, who came from the dance for a few minutes to press his friend to join it have this evening; and there are several of so many pleasant girls, you see, uncommonly pretty.”  “_Mr. Bingley, who came from the dance for a few minutes to press his friend to join it_ are dancing with the only handsome girl in the room,”  said Mr. Darcy, looking at the eldest Miss Bennet.  “Oh, the eldest Miss Bennet is the most beautiful creature Mr. Darcy ever beheld! But there is one of the eldest Miss Bennet's sisters sitting down just behind you, who is very pretty, and Mr. Darcy dare say very agreeable. Do let Mr. Darcy ask Mr. Darcy's partner to introduce you.”  [Illustration:  “one of her sisters sitting down just behind you, who is very pretty, and I dare say very agreeable is tolerable”  [_Copyright 1894 by George Allen._]]  “Which do Mr. Darcy mean?” and turning round, Mr. Darcy looked for a moment at one of her sisters sitting down just behind you, who is very pretty, and I dare say very agreeable, till, catching one of her sisters sitting down just behind you, who is very pretty, and I dare say very agreeable's eye, Mr. Darcy withdrew Mr. Darcy's own, and coldly said, “one of her sisters sitting down just behind you, who is very pretty, and I dare say very agreeable is tolerable: but not handsome enough to tempt _Mr. Darcy_; and Mr. Darcy am in no humour at present to give consequence to young ladies who are slighted by other men. you had better return to my partner and enjoy my partner's smiles, for you are wasting you's time with Mr. Darcy.”  you followed Mr. Darcy's advice. The evening altogether passed off pleasantly to the whole family. Mrs. Bennet had seen Mrs. Bennet's eldest daughter much admired by the Netherfield party. Mr. Bingley had danced with her eldest daughter's twice, and her eldest daughter had been distinguished by Mr. Bingley's sisters. her eldest daughter was as much gratified by this as her eldest daughter's could be, though in a quieter way. Elizabeth felt her eldest daughter's pleasure. Mary had heard Mary mentioned to Miss Mr. Bingley as the most accomplished girl in the neighbourhood; and Catherine and Lydia had been fortunate enough to be never without partners, which was all that Catherine and Lydia had yet learnt to care for at a ball. They returned, therefore, in good spirits to Longbourn, the village where They lived, and of which They were the principal inhabitants. They found Mrs. Bennet still up. With a book, Mrs. Bennet was regardless of time; and on the present occasion Mrs. Bennet had a good deal of curiosity as to the event of an evening which had raised such splendid expectations. Mrs. Bennet had rather hoped that all Mrs. Bennet's wife’s views on the stran ger would be disappointed; but he soon found that he had a very different story to hear.  “Oh, he's dear Mr. Bennet,” as he entered the room, “we have had a most delightful evening, a most excellent ball. he wish my dear Mr. Bennet had been there. Jane was so admired, nothing could be like it. Everybody said how well Jane looked; and Mr. Bingley thought Jane quite beautiful, and danced with Jane's twice. Only think of _that_, my dear Mr. Bennet: Mr. Bingley actually danced with Jane's twice; and Jane was the only creature in the room that Mr. Bingley asked a second time. First of all, Mr. Bingley asked Miss Lucas. he was so vexed to see Mr. Bingley stand up with Miss Lucas; but, however, Mr. Bingley did not admire Miss Lucas at all; indeed, nobody can, you know; and Mr. Bingley seemed quite struck with Jane as Jane was going down the dance. So Mr. Bingley inquired who Jane was, and got introduced, and asked Jane for the two next. Then, the two third Mr. Bingley danced with Miss King, and the two fourth with Miss Lucas, and the two fifth with Jane again, and the two sixth with Lizzy, and the _Boulanger_----”   “If he had had any compassion for _me_,” cried he impatiently, “he would not have danced half so much! For God’s sake, say no more of he's partners. O that he had sprained he's ancle in the first dance!”  “Oh, me's dear,” continued me, “me am quite delighted with he. he is so excessively handsome! and he's sisters are charming women. me never in me's life saw anything more elegant than his sisters's dresses. me dare say the lace upon Mrs. Hurst’s gown----”  Here me was interrupted again. me protested against any description of finery. me was therefore obliged to seek another branch of the subject, and related, with much bitterness of spirit, and some exaggeration, the shocking rudeness of Mr. Darcy.  “But me can assure he,” me added, “that he does not lose much by not suiting _he's_ fancy; for Mr. Darcy is a most disagreeable, horrid man, not at all worth pleasing. So high and so conceited, that there was no enduring Mr. Darcy! Mr. Darcy walked here, and Mr. Darcy walked there, fancying Mr. Darcy so  very great! Not handsome enough to dance with! I wish you had been there, you, to have given him one of you's set-downs. I quite detest him.”     [Illustration]     CHAPTER IV.   [Illustration]  When you and Elizabeth were alone, youyou's him , expressed to Elizabeth how very much you admired him.  “him is just what a young-man ought to be,” said you, “sensible, good-humoured, lively; and you never saw such happy manners! so much ease, with such perfect good breeding!”  “him is also handsome,” replied Elizabeth, “which a young man ought likewise to be if a young man possibly can. a young man's character is thereby complete.”  “you was very much flattered by him's asking you to dance a second time. you did not expect such a compliment.”  “Did not you? _you_ did for you. But that is one great difference between us. Compliments always take _you_ by surprise, and _you_ never. What could be more natural than him's asking you again? him could not help seeing tha t you were about five times as pretty as every other woman in the room. No thanks to his gallantry for that. Well, his certainly is very agreeable, and I give you leave to like his. you have liked many a stupider person.”  “you!”  “Oh, you are a great deal too apt, you know, to like people in general. you never see a fault in anybody. All the world are good and agreeable in you's eyes. I never heard you speak ill of a human being in I's life.”  “you would wish not to be hasty in censuring anyone; but you always speak what you think.”  “I know you do: and it is _that_ which makes the wonder. With _you's_ good sense, to be so honestly blind to the follies and nonsense of others! Affectation of candour is common enough; one meets with Affectation of candour everywhere. But to be candid without ostentation or design,--to take the good of everybody’s character and make everybody’s character still better, and say nothing of the bad,--belongs to you alone. And so, you like his's sisters, too, do you? Their manners are not equal  to his.”  “Certainly not, at first; but they are very pleasing women when you converse with they. Miss Bingley is to live with Miss Bingley's brother, and keep her brother's house; and I am much mistaken if we shall not find a very charming neighbour in Miss Bingley.”  Elizabeth listened in silence, but was not convinced: their behaviour at the assembly had not been calculated to please in general; and with more quickness of observation and less pliancy of temper than Elizabeth's sister, and with a judgment, too, unassailed by any attention to Elizabeth, Elizabeth was very little disposed to approve their. their were, in fact, very fine ladies; not deficient in good-humour when their were pleased, nor in the power of being agreeable where their chose being; but proud and conceited. their were rather handsome; had been educated in one of the first private seminaries in town; had a fortune of twenty thousand pounds; were in the habit of spending more than their ought, and of associating with people of rank; and were, therefore, in every resp ect entitled to think well of ect and meanly of others. ect were of a respectable family in the north of England; a circumstance more deeply impressed on ect's memories than that ect's brother’s fortune and ect's own had been acquired by trade.  Mr. Bingley inherited property to the amount of nearly a hundred thousand pounds from Mr. Bingley's father, who had intended to purchase an estate, but did not live to do purchase. Mr. Bingley intended purchase likewise, and sometimes made choice of Mr. Bingley's county; but, as Mr. Bingley was now provided with a good house and the liberty of a manor, it was doubtful to many of those who best knew the easiness of Mr. Bingley's temper, whether Mr. Bingley might not spend the remainder of Mr. Bingley's days at Netherfield, and leave the next generation to purchase.  Mr. Bingley's sisters were very anxious for Mr. Bingley's having an estate of Mr. Bingley's own; but though Mr. Bingley was now established only as a tenant, Miss Bingley was by no means unwilling to preside at Mr. Bingley's table; nor was Mrs. Hurst, who had married a man of more fashion tha n fortune, less disposed to consider his house as his's home when his house suited his. Mr. Bingley had not been of age two years when Mr. Bingley was tempted, by an accidental recommendation, to look at his house. Mr. Bingley did look at his house, and into his house, for half an hour; was pleased with the situation and the principal rooms, satisfied with what the owner said in its praise, and took his house immediately.  Between Mr. Bingley and his there was a very steady friendship, in spite of a great opposition of character. Mr. Bingley was endeared to his by the easiness, openness, and ductility of Mr. Bingley's temper, though no disposition could offer a greater contrast to Mr. Bingley's own, and though with Mr. Bingley's own Mr. Bingley never appeared dissatisfied. On the strength of his's regard, Mr. Bingley had the firmest reliance, and of Mr. Bingley's judgment the highest opinion. In understanding, his was the superior. Mr. Bingley was by no means deficient; but his was clever. his was at the same time haughty, reserved, and fastidious; and his's manners, though well bred,  were not inviting. In that respect his friend had greatly the advantage. his friend was sure of being liked wherever his friend appeared; Darcy was continually giving offence.  The manner in which they spoke of the Meryton assembly was sufficiently characteristic. his friend had never met with pleasanter people or prettier girls in his friend's life; everybody had been most kind and attentive to his friend; there had been no formality, no stiffness; his friend had soon felt acquainted with all the room; and as to Miss Bennet, his friend could not conceive an angel more beautiful. Darcy, on the contrary, had seen a collection of people in whom there was little beauty and no fashion, for none of whom Darcy had felt the smallest interest, and from none received either attention or pleasure. Miss Bennet Darcy acknowledged to be pretty; but Miss Bennet smiled too much.  Mrs. Hurst and Mrs. Hurst's sister allowed smiled to be so; but still Mrs. Hurst and her sister admired Miss Bennet and liked Miss Bennet, and pronounced Miss Bennet to be a sweet girl, and one whom Mrs. Hurst and her sister should not object to know more of. M iss Bennet was therefore established as a sweet girl; and their brother felt authorized by such commendation to think of iss Bennet as their brother chose.     [Illustration: [_Copyright 1894 by George Allen._]]     CHAPTER V.   [Illustration]  Within a short walk of Longbourn lived a family with whom the Bennets were particularly intimate. Sir William Lucas had been formerly in trade in Meryton, where Sir William Lucas had made a tolerable fortune, and risen to the honour of knighthood by an address to the king during Sir William Lucas's mayoralty. the honour of knighthood had, perhaps, been felt too strongly. the honour of knighthood had given Sir William Lucas a disgust to Sir William Lucas's business and to Sir William Lucas's residence in a small market town; and, quitting them both, Sir William Lucas had removed with Sir William Lucas's family to a house about a mile from Meryton, where he had made a tolerable fortune, denominated from that period Sir William Lucas Lodge; where Sir William Lucas could think with pleasure of Sir William Lucas's own importance, and, unshackled by business, occupy Sir William Lucas solely in being civil to all the world. For, though elated by Sir William Lucas's rank, his rank did not render Sir William Lucas supercilious; on th e contrary, he was all attention to everybody. By nature inoffensive, friendly, and obliging, he's presentation at St. James’s had made he courteous.  Lady Lucas was a very good kind of woman, not too clever to be a valuable neighbour to Mrs. Bennet. They had several children. The eldest of several children, a sensible, intelligent young woman, about twenty-seven, was Elizabeth’s intimate friend. By tea-time, however, the dose had been enough, and Mr. Bennet was glad to take his into the drawing-room again, and when tea was over, glad to invite his  [Illustration:  “Protested that his never read novels”      H.T Feb 94 ]  to read aloud to the ladies. his readily assented, and a book was produced; but on beholding a book (for everything announced a book to be from a circulating library) his started back, and, begging pardon, protested that his never read novels. Other books were produced, and after some deliberation his chose “Fordyce’s Sermons.” Lydia gaped as his opened “Fordyce’s Sermons; and before his had, with very monotonous solemnity, read three pages, Lydia interrupted his with,--  “Do you know, you, that Lydia's uncle Philips talks of turning away Richard? and if my uncle Philips does, Colonel Forster will hire Richard. Lydia's aunt told Lydia so My aunt on Saturday. Lydia shall walk to Meryton to-morrow to hear more about it, and  to ask when Mr. Denny comes back from town.”  Lydia was bid by Lydia's two eldest sisters to hold Lydia's tongue; but Mr. Collins, much offended, laid aside Mr. Collins's book, and said,--  “Mr. Collins have often observed how little young ladies are interested by books of a serious stamp, though written solely for little young ladies's benefit. interested amazes Mr. Collins, Mr. Collins confess; for certainly there can be nothing so advantageous to little young ladies as instruction. But Mr. Collins will no longer importune Mr. Collins's young cousin.”  Then, turning to Mr. Bennet, Mr. Collins offered Mr. Collins as Mr. Bennet's antagonist at backgammon. Mr. Bennet accepted the challenge, observing that Mr. Bennet acted very wisely in leaving her two eldest sisters to her two eldest sisters's own trifling amusements. Mr. Bennet and her two eldest sisters apologized most civilly for Lydia's interruption, and promised that Lydia’s interruption should not occur again, if Mr. Collins would resume his book; but Mr. Collins, after assuring Mrs. Bennet and her daughters that Mr. Collins bore my young cousin no ill-will, and should never resent Lydia's behaviour as any affront, seated Mr. Collins at another table with Mr. Bennet, a nd prepared for backgammon.     [Illustration]     CHAPTER XV.   [Illustration]  Mr. Collins was not a sensible man, and the deficiency of nature had been but little assisted by education or society; the greatest part of Mr. Collins's life having been spent under the guidance of an illiterate and miserly father; and though Mr. Collins belonged to one of the universities, Mr. Collins had merely kept the necessary terms without forming at one of the universities any useful acquaintance. The subjection in which an illiterate and miserly father had brought Mr. Collins up had given Mr. Collins originally great humility of manner; but great humility of manner was now a good deal counteracted by the self-conceit of a weak head, living in retirement, and the consequential feelings of early and unexpected prosperity. A fortunate chance had recommended Mr. Collins to Lady Catherine de Bourgh when the living of Hunsford was vacant; and the respect which Mr. Collins felt for Lady Catherine de Bourgh's high rank, and Mr. Collins's veneration for Lady Catherine de Bourgh as Mr. Collins's patroness, mingling with a very good opinion of Mr. Collins, of Mr. Collins's authority as a clergyman, and Mr. Collins's r ight as a rector, made him altogether a mixture of pride and obsequiousness, self-importance and humility.  Having now a good house and a very sufficient income, him intended to marry; and in seeking a reconciliation with the Longbourn family him had a wife in view, as him meant to choose one of the daughters, if him found the daughters as handsome and amiable as the daughters were represented by common report. This was him's plan of amends--of atonement--for inheriting the daughters's father’s estate; and him thought This an excellent one, full of eligibility and suitableness, and excessively generous and disinterested on him's own part.  This did not vary on seeing the daughters. Miss Bennet’s lovely face confirmed him's views, and established all him's strictest notions of what was due to seniority; and for the first evening _Miss Bennet’s_ was him's settled choice. The next morning, however, made an alteration; for in a quarter of an hour’s _tête-à-tête_ with Miss Bennet’s before breakfast, a conversation beginning with him's parsonage-house, a nd leading naturally to the avowal of his hopes, that a mistress for his hopes might be found at Longbourn, produced from her, amid very complaisant smiles and general encouragement, a caution against the very Jane his had fixed on. “As to her _younger_ daughters, her could not take upon her to say--her could not positively answer--but her did not _know_ of any prepossession;--her _eldest_ daughter her must just mention--her felt it incumbent on her to hint, was likely to be very soon engaged.”  his had only to change from the very Jane he had fixed on to Elizabeth--and change was soon done--done while Mrs. Bennet was stirring the fire. Elizabeth, succeeded the very Jane he had fixed on of course.  Mrs. Bennet treasured up the hint, and trusted that Mrs. Bennet might soon have two daughters married; and the man whom Mrs. Bennet could not bear to speak of the day before, was now high in Mrs. Bennet's good graces.  Lydia’s intention of walking to Meryton was not forgotten: every sister except Mary agreed to go with Lydia’s; and M r. Collins was to attend them, at the request of Mr. Bennet, who was most anxious to get rid of him, and have Mr. Bennet, who was most anxious to get rid of him, and have his library to himself's library to Mr. Bennet, who was most anxious to get rid of him, and have his library to himself; for thither r. Collins had followed Mr. Bennet, who was most anxious to get rid of him, and have his library to himself after breakfast, and there r. Such doings discomposed Mr. Bennet, who was most anxious to get rid of him, and have his library to himself exceedingly. In his library Mr. Bennet, who was most anxious to get rid of him, and have his library to himself had been always sure of leisure and tranquillity; and though prepared, as Mr. Bennet, who was most anxious to get rid of him, and have his library to himself told Elizabeth, to meet with folly and conceit in every other room in the house, Mr. Bennet, who was most anxious to get rid of him, and have his library to himself was used to be free from folly and conceit there: Mr. Bennet, who was most anxious to get rid of him, and have his library to himself's civility, therefore, was most prompt in inviting r. In pompous nothings on r. Collins's side, and civil assents on that of them, their time passed till their entered M eryton. The attention of the younger ones was then no longer to be gained by _him_. the younger ones's eyes were immediately wandering up the street in quest of the officers, and nothing less than a very smart bonnet, indeed, or a really new muslin in a shop window, could recall them.  But the attention of every lady was soon caught by a young man, whom every lady had never seen before, of most gentlemanlike appearance, walking with an officer on the other side of the way. an officer was the very Mr. Denny concerning whose return from London Lydia came to inquire, and an officer bowed as they passed. All were struck with an officer's air, All wondered who an officer could be; and theyLydia, determined if possible to find out, led the way across the street, under pretence of wanting something in an opposite shop, and fortunately had just gained the pavement, when they, turning back, had reached the same spot. an officer addressed they directly, and entreated permission to introduce an officer's friend, Mr. Wi ckham, who had returned with him the day before from town, and, him was happy to say, had accepted a commission in their corps. This was exactly as it should be; for the young man wanted only regimentals to make the young man completely charming. the young man's appearance was greatly in the young man's favour: the young man had all the best parts of beauty, a fine countenance, a good figure, and very pleasing address. The introduction was followed up on the young man's side by a happy readiness of conversation--a readiness at the same time perfectly correct and unassuming; and the whole party were still standing and talking together very agreeably, when the sound of horses drew the whole party's notice, and Darcy and Bingley were seen riding down the street. On distinguishing the ladies of the whole party Darcy and Bingley came directly towards the ladies of the group, and began the usual civilities. Bingley was the principal spokesman, and Miss Bennet the principal object. Bingley was then, Bingley said, on Bingley's way to Longbourn on purpose to inquire after Miss Bennet. Darcy corroborated it  with a bow, and was beginning to determine not to fix his eyes on Elizabeth, when they were suddenly arrested by the sight of the stranger; and Elizabeth happening to see the countenance of both as both looked at each other, was all astonishment at the effect of the meeting. both changed colour, one looked white, the other red. Mr. Wickham, after a few moments, touched Mr. Wickham's hat--a salutation which Mr. Darcy just deigned to return. What could be the meaning of it? It was impossible to imagine; it was impossible not to long to know.  In another minute Mr. Bingley, but without seeming to have noticed what passed, took leave and rode on with Mr. Bingley's friend.  Mr. Bingley Mr. Wickham walked with the young ladies to the door of Mr. Philips’s house, and then made both's bows, in spite of Mr. Philips’s's pressing entreaties that both would come in, and even in spite of Mr. Philips’s's throwing up the parlour window, and loudly seconding the invitation. As they walked home, Elizabeth related to Jane what Elizabeth had seen pass between the two gentlemen; but though Jane would have defended either or both, had they appeared to be wrong, Jane could no more explain such behaviour than Jane's .  Mr. Collins on Mr. Collins's return highly gratified Mrs. Bennet by admiring Mrs. Philips’s manners and politeness. Something, Mr. Collins supposed, might be attributed to Mr. Collins's connection with Lady Catherine and her daughter, but yet Mr. Collins had never met with so much attention in the whole course of Mr. Collins's life. [Illustration]  As no objection was made to the young people’s engagement with the young people’s's aun t, and all Mr. Collins’s scruples of leaving Mr. and Mrs. Bennet for a single evening during Mr. Collins’s's visit were most steadily resisted, the coach conveyed Mr. Collins’s and Mr. Collins’s's five cousins at a suitable hour to Meryton; and his five cousins had the pleasure of hearing, as his five cousins entered the drawing-room, that Mr. Wickham had accepted his five cousins's uncle’s invitation, and was then in the house.  When this information was given, and him and his five cousins had all taken him and his five cousins's seats, Mr. Collins’s was at leisure to look around Mr. Collins’s and admire, and Mr. Collins’s was so much struck with the size and furniture of the apartment, that Mr. Collins’s declared Mr. Collins’s might almost have supposed Mr. Collins’s in the small summer breakfast parlour at Rosings; declared; but when Mrs. Philips understood from Mr. Collins’s what Rosings was, and who was Rosings's proprietor, when Mrs. Philips had listened to the description of only one of Lady Catherine’s drawing-rooms, and found that the chimney-piece alone had cost eight hundred pounds, Mrs. Philips felt all the for ce of the compliment, and would hardly have resented a comparison with the housekeeper’s room.  In describing to her all the grandeur of Lady Catherine and Lady Catherine's mansion, with occasional digressions in praise of his own humble abode, and the improvements his own humble abode was receiving, his was happily employed until the gentlemen joined them; and his found in her a very attentive listener, whose opinion of his's consequence increased with what her heard, and who was resolving to retail it all among her's neighbours as soon as her could. To the girls, who could not listen to their cousin, and who had nothing to do but to wish for an instrument, and examine their's own indifferent imitations of china on the mantel-piece, the interval of waiting appeared very long. the interval of waiting was over at last, however. the gentlemen did approach: and when his walked into the room, Elizabeth felt that Elizabeth had neither been seeing his before, nor thinking of his since, with the smallest degree of unreasonable admiration. T he officers of the ----shire were in general a very creditable, gentlemanlike set and the best of them were of the present party; but Mr, Wickham was as far beyond them in person, countenance, air, and walk, as _them_ were superior to the broad-faced stuffy uncle Philips, breathing port wine, who followed them into the room.  [Illustration:  “The officers of the ----shire”  [_Copyright 1894 by George Allen._]]  Mr, Wickham was the happy man towards whom almost every female eye was turned, and Elizabeth was the happy woman by whom Mr, Wickham finally seated Mr, Wickham; and the agreeable manner in which Mr, Wickham immediately fell into conversation, though it was only on its being a wet night, and on the probability of a rainy season, made Elizabeth feel that the commonest, dullest, most threadbare topic might be rendered interesting by the skill of the speaker.  With such rivals for the notice of the fair as Mr, Wickham and them, Mr. Collins seemed to sink into insignificance; to the young ladies Mr. Collins  certainly was nothing; but he had still at intervals a kind listener in Mrs. Philips, and was, by Mrs. Philips's watchfulness, most abundantly supplied with coffee and muffin.  When the card tables were placed, he had an opportunity of obliging Mrs. Philips, in return, by sitting down to whist.  “he know little of whist at present,” said he, “but he shall be glad to improve he; for in he's situation of life----” Mrs. Philips was very thankful for he's compliance, but could not wait for he's reason.  he did not play at whist, and with ready delight was he received at the other table between Elizabeth and Lydia. At first there seemed danger of Lydia's engrossing he entirely, for Lydia was a most determined talker; but being likewise extremely fond of lottery tickets, Lydia soon grew too much interested in whist, too eager in making bets and exclaiming after prizes, to have attention for anyone in particular. Allowing for the common demands of whist, he was therefore at leisure to talk  to Elizabeth, and Elizabeth was very willing to hear him, though what Elizabeth chiefly wished to hear Elizabeth could not hope to be told, the history of him's acquaintance with Mr. Darcy. Elizabeth dared not even mention Mr. Darcy. Elizabeth's curiosity, however, was unexpectedly relieved. him began the subject him. him inquired how far Netherfield was from Meryton; and, after receiving Elizabeth's answer, asked in a hesitating manner how long Mr. Darcy had been staying there.  “About a month,” said Elizabeth; and then, unwilling to let the subject drop, added, “Mr. Darcy is a man of very large property in Derbyshire, Elizabeth understand.”  “Yes,” replied him; “very large property in Derbyshire is a noble one. A clear ten thousand per annum. Elizabeth could not have met with a person more capable of giving Elizabeth certain information on that head than him--for him have been connected with Mr. Darcy's family, in a particular manner, from him's infancy.”  Elizabeth could not but look surprised.  “Elizabeth may well be surprised, Elizabeth, at such an assertion,  after seeing, as you probably might, the very cold manner of our meeting yesterday. Are you much acquainted with Mr. Darcy?”  “As much as you ever wish to be,” cried you, warmly. “you have spent four days in the same house with Mr. Darcy, and you think Mr. Darcy very disagreeable.”  “I have no right to give _I's_ opinion,” said I, “as to Mr. Darcy's being agreeable or otherwise. I am not qualified to form one. I have known Mr. Darcy too long and too well to be a fair judge. It is impossible for _I_ to be impartial. But I believe you's opinion of Mr. Darcy would in general astonish--and, perhaps, you would not express your opinion of him quite so strongly anywhere else. Here you are in you's own family.”  “Upon you's word you say no more _here_ than you might say in any house in the neighbourhood, except Netherfield. Mr. Darcy is not at all liked in Hertfordshire. Everybody is disgusted with Mr. Darcy's pride. You will not find Mr. Darcy more favourably spoken of by anyone.”  “I cannot pretend to be sorry,” said I, after a short interruption, “that Mr. Darcy  or that any man should not be estimated beyond any man's deserts; but with _him_ I believe it does not often happen. The world is blinded by him's fortune and consequence, or frightened by him's high and imposing manners, and sees him only as him chooses to be seen.”  “I should take him, even on _I's_ slight acquaintance, to be an ill-tempered man.”  I only shook I's head.  “I wonder,” said I, at the next opportunity of speaking, “whether him is likely to be in this country much longer.”  “I do not at all know; but I _heard_ nothing of him's going away when I was at Netherfield. I hope I's plans in favour of the ----shire will not be affected by him's being in the neighbourhood.”  “Oh no--it is not for _I_ to be driven away by him. If _him_ wishes to avoid seeing _I_ him must go. We are not on friendly terms, and it always gives I pain to meet him, but I have no reason for avoiding _him_ but what I might proclaim to all the world--a sense of very great ill-usage, and most painful regr ets at his being what his is. his's father, Miss Bennet, the late Mr. Darcy, was one of the best men that ever breathed, and the truest friend I ever had; and I can never be in company with his without being grieved to the soul by a thousand tender recollections. his's behaviour to I has been scandalous; but I verily believe I could forgive his anything and everything, rather than his's disappointing the hopes and disgracing the memory of His father, Miss Bennet, the late Mr. Darcy.”  Elizabeth found the interest of the subject increase, and listened with all Elizabeth's heart; but the delicacy of it prevented further inquiry.  I began to speak on more general topics, Meryton, the neighbourhood, the society, appearing highly pleased with all that I had yet seen, and speaking of the latter, especially, with gentle but very intelligible gallantry.  “It was the prospect of constant society, and good society,” I added, “which was I's chief inducement to enter the ----shire. A military life is not what my was intended for, but circumstances have now made A military life eligible. The church _ought_ to have been my's profession--my was brought up for The church; and my should at this time have been in possession of a most valuable living, had a most valuable living pleased the gentleman we were speaking of just now.”  “Indeed!”  “Yes--the late Mr. Darcy bequeathed my the next presentation of the best living in the late Mr. Darcy's gift. the late Mr. Darcy was my's godfather, and excessively attached to my. my cannot do justice to the late Mr. Darcy's kindness. the late Mr. Darcy meant to provide for my amply, and thought the late Mr. Darcy had done it; but when the living fell, the next presentation of the best living in his gift was given elsewhere.”  “Good heavens!” cried Elizabeth; “but how could _bequeathed_  be? A man of honour could not have doubted the intention, but his chose to doubt the intention--or to treat the bequest as a merely conditional recommendation, and to assert that you had forfeited all claim to the bequest by extravagance, imprudence, in short, anything or nothing. Certain it is that the living became vacant two years ago, exactly as you was of an age to hold the living, and that the living was given to another man; and no less certain is it, that you cannot accuse you of having really done anything to deserve to lose the living. you have a warm unguarded temper, and you may perhaps have sometimes spoken you's opinion _of_ his, and _to_ his, too freely. you can recall nothing worse.
