Till me can forget me's father, me can never defy or expose _his father_.”  Elizabeth honoured me for such feelings, and thought me handsomer than ever as me expressed such feelings.  “But what,” said Elizabeth, after a pause, “can have been his father's motive? what can have induced his father to behave so cruelly?”  “A thorough, determined dislike of me--a dislike which me cannot but attribute in some measure to jealousy. Had his father liked me less, his father's might have borne with me better; but his father's uncommon attachment to me irritated me, me believe, very early in life. me had not a temper to bear the sort of competition in which we stood--the sort of preference which was often given me.”  “me had not thought his father so bad as this--though me have never liked his father, me had not thought so very ill of his father--me had supposed his father to be despising his father's fellow-creatures in general, but did not suspect his father of descending to such malicious revenge, such injustice, such inhumanity as this!”  Afte r a few minutes’ reflection, however, she continued, “she _do_ remember his boasting one day, at Netherfield, of the implacability of his's resentments, of his's having an unforgiving temper. his's disposition must be dreadful.”  “I will not trust I on the subject,” replied I; “_I_ can hardly be just to his.”  she was again deep in thought, and after a time exclaimed, “To treat in such a manner the godson, the friend, the favourite of the godson, the friend, the favourite of his father's father!” she could have added, “A young man, too, like _I_, whose very countenance may vouch for I's being amiable.” But she contented she with--“And one, too, who had probably been his's own companion from childhood, connected together, as she think I said, in the closest manner.”  “We were born in the same parish, within the same park; the greatest part of We's youth was passed together: inmates of the same house, sharing the same amusements, objects of the same parental care. _his began life in the profession which I's uncle, M r. There could be no conversation in the noise of Mrs. Philips’s supper party, but h's manners recommended h to everybody. Whatever h said, was said well; and whatever h did, done gracefully. The rest of the evening brought Mr. Darcy's little amusement. [Illustration:       “Protested he must be entirely mistaken.”  [_Copyright 1894 by George Allen._]]     CHAPTER XXIII. [Illustration]  With no greater events than these in the Longbourn family, and otherwise diversified by little beyond the walks to Meryton, sometimes dirty and sometimes cold, did January and February pass away.  March was to take Elizabeth to Hunsford. Elizabeth had not at first thought very seriously of going thither; but Charlotte, Elizabeth soon found, was depending on the plan, and Elizabeth gradually learned to consider the plan Elizabeth with greater pleasure as well as greater certainty. Absence had increased Elizabeth's desire of seeing Charlotte again, and weakened Elizabeth's disgust of Mr. Collins. There was novelty in the plan; and as, with such a mother and such uncompanionable sisters, home could not be faultless, a little change was not unwelcome for a little change's own sake. The journey would, moreover, give Elizabeth a peep at Jane; and, in short, as the time drew near, Elizabeth would have been very sorry for any delay. Everything, however, went on smoothly, and was finally settled according to Charlotte's first sketch. Elizabeth was to accompany Sir William and Sir William's second daughter. The improvement of spending a night in London was added in time, and the plan became as perfect as plan could be.  The only pain was in leaving Elizabeth's father, who wou ld certainly miss her, and who, when it came to the point, so little liked her going, that he told her to write to he, and almost promised to answer her's letter.  The farewell between her and he was perfectly friendly; on he's side even more. he's present pursuit could not make he forget that her had been the first to excite and to deserve he's attention, the first to listen and to pity, the first to be admired; and in he's manner of bidding her adieu, wishing her every enjoyment, reminding her of what her was to expect in Lady Catherine de Bourgh, and trusting their opinion of Lady Catherine de Bourgh--their's opinion of everybody--would always coincide, there was a solicitude, an interest, which her felt must ever attach her to he with a most sincere regard; and her parted from he convinced, that, whether married or single, he must always be her's model of the amiable and pleasing.  her's fellow-travellers the next day were not of a kind to make her think he less agreeable. Sir William Lucas , and his daughter Maria, a good-humoured girl, but as empty-headed as his, had nothing to say that could be worth hearing, and were listened to with about as much delight as the rattle of the chaise. Elizabeth loved absurdities, but Elizabeth had known his's too long. his could tell Elizabeth nothing new of the wonders of his's presentation and knighthood; and his's civilities were worn out, like his's information.  It was a journey of only twenty-four miles, and they began It so early as to be in Gracechurch Street by noon. As they drove to Mr. Gardiner’s door, Jane was at a drawing-room window watching they's arrival: when they entered the passage, Jane was there to welcome they, and Elizabeth, looking earnestly in Elizabeth's face, was pleased to see her face healthful and lovely as ever. On the stairs were a troop of little boys and girls, whose eagerness for their cousin’s appearance would not allow their to wait in the drawing-room, and whose shyness, as their had not seen their cousin’s for a twelvemonth, prevent ed their coming lower. All was joy and kindness. The day passed most pleasantly away; the morning in bustle and shopping, and the evening at one of the theatres.  Elizabeth then contrived to sit by Elizabeth's aunt. Their first subject was Elizabeth's sister; and Elizabeth was more grieved than astonished to hear, in reply to Elizabeth's minute inquiries, that though her sister always struggled to support her sister's spirits, there were periods of dejection. It was reasonable, however, to hope that Their would not continue long. her aunt gave Elizabeth the particulars also of Miss Bingley’s visit in Gracechurch Street, and repeated conversations occurring at different times between her sister and Elizabeth, which proved that Elizabeth had, from Elizabeth's heart, given up the acquaintance.  her aunt then rallied her aunt's on Wickham’s desertion, and complimented Elizabeth on bearing Wickham’s desertion so well.  “But, her aunt's ,” her aunt added, “what sort of girl is Miss Bingley’s? her aunt should be sorry to think Wickham’s mercenary.”  “Pray, her aunt, what is the  difference in matrimonial affairs, between the mercenary and the prudent motive? Where does discretion end, and avarice begin? Last Christmas you were afraid of his marrying me, because marrying would be imprudent; and now, because his is trying to get a girl with only ten thousand pounds, you want to find out that his is mercenary.”  “If you will only tell me what sort of girl a girl with only ten thousand pounds is, me shall know what to think.”  “a girl with only ten thousand pounds is a very good kind of girl, you believe. you know no harm of a girl with only ten thousand pounds.”  “But his paid a girl with only ten thousand pounds not the smallest attention till a girl with only ten thousand pounds's grandfather’s death made a girl with only ten thousand pounds's mistress of this fortune?”  “No--why should his? If it were not allowable for his to gain _me's_ affections, because me had no money, what occasion could there be for making love to a girl whom his did not care about, and who was equally poor?”  “But there seems indelicacy in directing his's attentions towards a girl with only ten thousand pounds so soon after this event.”  “A man in distressed circumstances has not time for all those elegant decorums which other pe ople may observe. If _she_ does not object to it, why should _we_?”  “_she's_ not objecting does not justify _him_. _Her_ not objecting only shows she being deficient in something she--sense or feeling.”  “Well,” cried Elizabeth, “have it as you choose. _him_ shall be mercenary, and _she_ shall be foolish.”  “No, Elizabeth, that is what you do _not_ choose. you should be sorry, Elizabeth know, to think ill of him.”  “Oh, if that is all, Elizabeth have a very poor opinion of young men who live in Derbyshire; and young men who live in Derbyshire's intimate friends who live in Hertfordshire are not much better. Elizabeth am sick of them all. Thank heaven! Elizabeth am going to-morrow where Elizabeth shall find a man who has not one agreeable quality, who has neither manners nor sense to recommend a man who has not one agreeable quality, who has neither manners nor sense to recommend him. Stupid men are the only ones worth knowing, after all.”  “Take care, Elizabeth; that speech savours strongly of disappointment.”  Before they were separated by the conclusion of the play, Elizabeth had the unexpected happiness of an invitation to  accompany her uncle and aunt in a tour of pleasure which her uncle and aunt proposed taking in the summer.  “We have not quite determined how far it shall carry We,” said Mrs. Gardiner; “but perhaps, to the Lakes.”  No scheme could have been more agreeable to her, and her's acceptance of the invitation was most ready and grateful. “her's dear, dear aunt,” her rapturously cried, “what delight! what felicity! My dear, dear aunt give her fresh life and vigour. Adieu to disappointment and spleen. What are men to rocks and mountains? Oh, what hours of transport We shall spend! And when We _do_ return, it shall not be like other travellers, without being able to give one accurate idea of anything. We _will_ know where We have gone--We _will_ recollect what We have seen. Lakes, mountains, and rivers, shall not be jumbled together in We's imaginations; nor, when We attempt to describe any particular scene, will We begin quarrelling about any particular scene's relative situation. Let _We's_ first effusions be less insupportable than those of  the generality of travellers.”     [Illustration:       “At the door” ]     CHAPTER XXVIII.   [Illustration]  Every object in the next day’s journey was new and interesting to Elizabeth; and Elizabeth's spirits were in a state of enjoyment; for Elizabeth had seen Elizabeth's sister looking so well as to banish all fear for her sister's health, and the prospect of her sister's northern tour was a constant source of delight.  When they left the high road for the lane to Hunsford, every eye was in search of the Parsonage, and every turning expected to bring the Parsonage in view. The paling of Rosings park was they's boundary on one side. Elizabeth smiled at the recollection of all that Elizabeth had heard of Rosings park's inhabitants.  At length the Parsonage was discernible. The garden sloping to the road, the Parsonage standing in it, the green pales and the laurel hedge, everything declared they were arriving. Mr. Collins and Charlotte appeared at the door, and the carriage stopped at the small gate, which led by a short gravel walk to the Parsonage, amidst t he nods and smiles of the whole party. In a moment the whole party were all out of the chaise, rejoicing at the sight of each other. Elizabeth soon perceived, that though her Ladyship was not in the commission of the peace for the county, her Ladyship was a most active magistrate in her Ladyship's own parish, the minutest concerns of which were carried to her Ladyship by Mrs. Collins’s; and whenever any of the cottag ers were disposed to be quarrelsome, discontented, or too poor, she sallied forth into the village to settle their differences, silence their's complaints, and scold their into harmony and plenty.  [Illustration:       “he never failed to inform them” ]  The entertainment of dining at Rosings was repeated about twice a week; and, allowing for the loss of Sir William, and there being only one card-table in the evening, every such entertainment was the counterpart of the first. In this perturbed state of mind, with thoughts that could rest on nothing, her walked on; but it would not do: in half a minute the whole letter was unfolded again; and collecting her as well as her could, her again began the mortifying perusal of all that related to his, and commanded her so far as to examine the meaning of every sentence. So far each recital confirmed the other; but when she came to the will, the difference was great. What he had said of the living was fresh in she's memory; and as she recalled he's very words, it was impossible not to feel that there was gross duplicity on one side or the other, and, for a few moments, she flattered she that she's wishes did not err. On both sides it was only assertion. Again sh e read on. But every line proved more clearly that the affair, which she had believed it impossible that any contrivance could so represent as to render Mr. Darcy’s conduct in the affair, which she had believed it impossible that any contrivance could so represent as to render Mr. Darcy’s conduct in it less than infamous less than infamous, was capable of a turn which must make Mr. Darcy’s entirely blameless throughout the whole. Of Mr. Darcy’s's former way of life, nothing had been known in Hertfordshire but what Mr. Darcy’s told  [Illustration:       “Meeting accidentally in Town”  [_Copyright 1894 by George Allen._]]  Mr. Darcy’s. As to Mr. Darcy’s's real character, had information been in she's power, she had never felt a wish of inquiring. Mr. Darcy’s's countenance, voice, and manner,  had established him at once in the possession of every virtue. She grew absolutely ashamed of She. How humiliating is this discovery! Yet, how just a humiliation! Had She been in love, She could not have been more wretchedly blind. But vanity, not love, has been She's folly. Till this moment, She never knew She.”  From She to Jane, from Jane to Mr. Bingley, She's thoughts were in a  line which soon brought to her recollection that Mr. Darcy’s explanation _there_ had appeared very insufficient; and her read it again. [Illustration:       “How nicely we are crammed in” ]     CHAPTER XXXIX.   [Illustration]  It was the second week in May, in which the three young ladies set out together from Gracechurch Street for the town of ----, in Hertfordshire; and, as the three young ladies drew near the appointed inn where Mr. Bennet’s carriage was to meet the three young ladies, the three young ladies quickly perceived, in token of the coachman’s punctuality, both Kitty and Lydia looking out of a dining-room  upstairs. [Illustration:  The arrival of the Gardiners ]     CHAPTER XLII.   [Illustration]  Had Elizabeth’s opinion been all drawn from Elizabeth’s's own family, Elizabeth’s could not have formed a very pleasing picture of conjugal felicity or domestic comfort. Elizabeth’s's father, captivated by youth and beauty, and that appearance of good-humour which youth and beauty generally give, had married a woman whose weak understanding and illiberal mind had very early in their marriage put an end to all real affection for a woman whose weak understanding and illiberal mind had very early in their marriage put an end to all real affection for her. Respect, esteem, and confidence had vanished for ever; and all Her father's views of domestic happiness were overthrown. But Her father was not of a disposition to seek comfort for the disappointment which Her father's own imprudence had brought on in any of those pleasures which too often console the unfortunate for the unfortunate's folly or the unfortunate's vice. Her father was fond of the country and of books; and from these tastes had arisen Her father's principal enjoyments. To Her father's wife Her father was very little otherwise indebted than as Elizabeth’s i gnorance and folly had contributed to his amusement. This is not the sort of happiness which a man would in general wish to owe to a man's wife; but where other powers of entertainment are wanting, the true philosopher will derive benefit from such as are given.  Elizabeth, however, had never been blind to the impropriety of Elizabeth's father’s behaviour as a husband. Elizabeth had always seen the impropriety of her father’s behaviour as a husband with pain; but respecting her father’s's abilities, and grateful for her father’s's affectionate treatment of Elizabeth, Elizabeth endeavoured to forget what Elizabeth could not overlook, and to banish from Elizabeth's thoughts that continual breach of conjugal obligation and decorum which, in exposing her father’s's wife to the contempt of his wife's own children, was so highly reprehensible. A scheme of which every part promises delight can never be successful; and general disappointment is only warded off by the defence of some little peculiar vexat ion.”  When Lydia went away Lydia promised to write very often and very minutely to Lydia's mother and Kitty; but Lydia's letters were always long expected, and always very short. After the first fortnight or three weeks of Lydia's absence, health, good-humour, and cheerfulness began to reappear at Longbourn. Everything wore a happier aspect. The families who had been in town for the winter came back again, an d summer finery and summer engagements arose. Mrs. Bennet was restored to Mrs. Bennet's usual querulous serenity; and by the middle of June Kitty was so much recovered as to be able to enter Meryton without tears,--an event of such happy promise as to make Elizabeth hope, that by the following Christmas Elizabeth might be so tolerably reasonable as not to mention an officer above once a day, unless, by some cruel and malicious arrangement at the War Office, another regiment should be quartered in Meryton.  The time fixed for the beginning of their northern tour was now fast approaching; and a fortnight only was wanting of it, when a letter arrived from Mrs. Gardiner, which at once delayed their northern tour's commencement and curtailed their northern tour's extent. Mrs. Gardiner would be prevented by business from setting out till a fortnight later in July, and must be in London again within a month; and as that left too short a period for their to go so far, and see so much as their had proposed, or at least to see it with the leisure an d comfort they had built on, they were obliged to give up the Lakes, and substitute a more contracted tour; and, according to the present plan, were to go no farther northward than Derbyshire. In Derbyshire there was enough to be seen to occupy the chief of they's three weeks; and to Mrs. Gardiner Derbyshire had a peculiarly strong attraction. The town where Mrs. Gardiner had formerly passed some years of Mrs. Gardiner's life, and where they were now to spend a few days, was probably as great an object of Mrs. Gardiner's curiosity as all the celebrated beauties of Matlock, Chatsworth, Dovedale, or the Peak. Every day at Longbourn was now a day of anxiety; but the most anxious part of each was when the post was expected. The arrival of letters was the first grand object of every morning’s impatience. [Illustration]  Mr. Bennet had very often wished, before this period of Mr. Bennet's life, that, instead of spending Mr. Bennet's whole income, Mr. Bennet had laid by an annual sum, for the better provision of Mr. Bennet's children, and of Mr. Bennet's , if her sister survived Mr. Bennet. Mr. Bennet now wished it more than ever. Had Mr. Bennet done Mr. Bennet's duty in that respect, her sister need not have been indebted to her sister's uncle for whatever of honour or credit could now be purchased for her sister. The satisfaction of prevailing on one of the most worthless young men in Great Britain to be her sister's husband might then have rested in The satisfaction of prevailing on one of the most worthless young men in Great Britain to be her husband's proper place.  Mr. Bennet was seriously concerned that a cause of so little advantage to anyone should be forwarded at the sole expense of Mr. Bennet's brother-in-law; and h e was determined, if possible, to find out the extent of his assistance, and to discharge the obligation as soon as his could.  When first Mr. Bennet had married, economy was held to be perfectly useless; for, of course, they were to have a son. a son was to join in cutting off the entail, as soon as a son should be of age, and the widow and younger children would by that means be provided for. Five daughters successively entered the world, but yet a son was to come; and Mr. Bennet, for many years after Lydia’s birth, had been certain that a son would. This event had at last been despaired of, but it was then too late to be saving. Mr. Bennet had no turn for economy; and Mr. Bennet's love of independence had alone prevented they's exceeding they's income.  Five thousand pounds was settled by marriage articles on Mr. Bennet and the children. But in what proportions Five thousand pounds should be divided amongst the children depended on the will of the parents. This was one point, with regard to Lydia at le ast, which was now to be settled, and Mr. Bennet could have no hesitation in acceding to the proposal before Mr. Bennet. In terms of grateful acknowledgment for the kindness of Mr. Bennet's brother, though expressed most concisely, Mr. Bennet then delivered on paper Mr. Bennet's perfect approbation of all that was done, and Mr. Bennet's willingness to fulfil the engagements that had been made for Mr. Bennet. Mr. Bennet had never before supposed that, could his brother be prevailed on to marry Mr. Bennet's daughter, marry would be done with so little inconvenience to Mr. Bennet as by the present arrangement. Mr. Bennet would scarcely be ten pounds a year the loser, by the hundred that was to be paid them; for, what with his daughter's board and pocket allowance, and the continual presents in money which passed to his daughter through his daughter's mother’s hands, his daughter's expenses had been very little within that sum. No sentiment of shame gave a damp to Mrs. Bennet's triumph. You will only think you feel _more_ than you ought to do when you tell You all.”  “What do you mean?”  “Why, you must confess that you love him better than you do Bingley. you am afraid You will be angry.”  “You's , now be, _be_ serious. You want to talk very seriously. Let You know everything that You am to know without delay. Will you tell You how long you have loved him?”  “loved has been coming on so gradually, that you hardly know when loved began; but you believe you must date loved from you's first seeing him's beautiful grounds at Pemberley.”  Another entreaty that you wou ld be serious, however, produced the desired effect; and she soon satisfied Jane by she's solemn assurances of attachment. When convinced on that article, Miss Bennet had nothing further to wish.  “Now Miss Bennet am quite happy,” said Miss Bennet, “for she will be as happy as Miss Bennet. Miss Bennet always had a value for him. Were it for nothing but him's love of she, Miss Bennet must always have esteemed him; but now, as him's friend and she's husband, there can be only him and she more dear to Miss Bennet. But, she, she have been very sly, very reserved with me. How little did she tell me of what passed at Pemberley and Lambton! me owe all that me know of it to another, not to she.”  she told me the motives of she's secrecy. she had been unwilling to mention him; and the unsettled state of she's own feelings had made she equally avoid the name of him: but now she would no longer conceal from me him's share in Lydia’s marriage. What can that disagreeable Mr. Darcy mean by being so tiresome as to be always coming here? Mrs. Bennet had no notion but that disagreeable Mr. Darcy would go a-shooting, or something or other, and not disturb our with that disagreeable Mr. Darcy's company. What shall our do with that disagreeable Mr. Darcy? Lizzy, Lizzy must walk out with our dear Bingley again, that that disagreeable Mr. Darcy may not be in our dear Bingley's way.”  Lizzy could hardly help laughing at so convenient a proposal; yet was really vexed that Lizzy's should be always giving our dear Bingley such an epithet.  As soon as they entered, our dear Bingley looked at Lizzy so expressively, and shook hands with such warmth, as left no doubt of our dear Bingley's good information; and our dear Bingley soon afterwards said aloud, “Mrs. Bennet, have Mrs. Bennet no more lanes hereabouts in which Lizzy may lose Lizzy's way again to-day?”  “Mrs. Bennet advise that disagreeable Mr. Darcy, and Lizzy, and Kitty,” said Mrs. Bennet, “to walk to Oakham Mount this morning. walk is a nice long walk, and that disagreeable Mr. Darcy has never seen the view. ”  “It may do very well for the others,” replied Mr. Bingley; “but Mr. Bingley am sure It will be too much for Kitty. Won’t It, Kitty?”  Kitty owned that Kitty had rather stay at home. Darcy professed a great curiosity to see the view from the Mount, and Elizabeth silently consented. As Elizabeth went upstairs to get ready, Mrs. Bennet followed Elizabeth, saying,--  “Mrs. Bennet am quite sorry, Elizabeth, that Elizabeth should be forced to have that disagreeable man all to Elizabeth; but Mrs. Bennet hope Elizabeth will not mind have. It is all for Jane’s sake, Elizabeth know; and there is no occasion for talking to that disagreeable man except just now and then; so do not put Elizabeth to inconvenience.”  During their walk, it was resolved that Mrs. Bennet's consent should be asked in the course of the evening: Elizabeth reserved to Elizabeth the application for Mrs. Bennet's. Elizabeth could not determine how Mrs. Bennet would take the application for her mother’s; sometimes doubting whether all that disagreeable man's wealth and grandeur would be enough to overcome Mrs. Bennet's abhorrence of that disagreeable man; but whether Elizabeth were violently set aga inst the match, or violently delighted with the match, it was certain that her manner would be equally ill adapted to do credit to her's sense; and her could no more bear that Mr. Darcy should hear the first raptures of her's joy, than the first vehemence of her's disapprobation.  In the evening, soon after Mr. Bennet withdrew to the library, her saw Mr. Darcy rise also and follow Mr. Bennet, and her's agitation on seeing it was extreme. her did not fear Mr. Bennet's opposition, but Mr. Bennet was going to be made unhappy, and that made should be through her's means; that _herMr. Bennet's , should be distressing Mr. Bennet by her's choice, should be filling Mr. Bennet with fears and regrets in disposing of her, was a wretched reflection, and her sat in misery till Mr. Darcy appeared again, when, looking at Mr. Darcy, her was a little relieved by Mr. Darcy's smile. In a few minutes Mr. Darcy approached the table where her was sitting with Kitty; and, while pretending to admire her's work, said in a whisper, “Go to Mr. Bennet; Mr. Bennet wants her in the li brary.” She was gone directly.  She's father was walking about the room, looking grave and anxious. “She,” said Her father, “what are She doing? Are She out of She's senses to be accepting this man? Have not She always hated this man?”  How earnestly did She then wish that She's former opinions had been more reasonable, She's expressions more moderate! It would have spared She from explanations and professions which it was exceedingly awkward to give; but explanations and professions which it was exceedingly awkward to give were now necessary, and She assured Her father, with some confusion, of She's attachment to this man.  “Or, in other words, She are determined to have this man. this man is rich, to be sure, and She may have more fine clothes and fine carriages than Jane. But will more fine clothes and fine carriages than Jane make She happy?”  “Have Her father any other objection,” said She, “than Her father's belief of She's indifference?”  “None at all. We all know this man to be a proud, unpleasant sort of man; but this would be nothing if She really liked this man.”  “She do, She do like this man,” She replied, with tears in She's eyes; “She love this man. She ndeed he has no improper pride. he is perfectly amiable. You do not know what he really is; then pray do not pain me by speaking of he in such terms.”  “me,” said You, “You have given he You's consent. he is the kind of man, indeed, to whom You should never dare refuse anything, which he condescended to ask. You now give it to _me_, if me are resolved on having he. But let You advise me to think better of give. You know me's disposition, me. You know that me could be neither happy nor respectable, unless me truly esteemed me's , unless me looked up to he as a superior. me's lively talents would place me in the greatest danger in an unequal marriage. me could scarcely escape discredit and misery. You's , let You not have the grief of seeing _me_ unable to respect me's . me know not what me are about.”  Elizabeth, still more affected, was earnest and solemn in me's reply; and, at length, by repeated assurances that he was really the object of me's  choice, by explaining the gradual change which her estimation of him had undergone, relating her's absolute certainty that him's affection was not the work of a day, but had stood the test of many months’ suspense, and enumerating with energy all him's good qualities, her did conquer her's father’s incredulity, and reconcile her father’s to the match.  “Well, her father’s's ,” said her father’s, when her ceased speaking, “her father’s have no more to say. If this be the case, him deserves her. her father’s could not have parted with her, her father’s's , to anyone less worthy.”  To complete the favourable impression, her then told her father’s what Mr. Darcy had voluntarily done for Lydia. her father’s heard her with astonishment.  “This is an evening of wonders, indeed! And so, Mr. Darcy did everything; made up the match, gave the money, paid him's debts, and got him him's commission! So much the better. It will save her father’s a world of trouble and economy. Had it been her's uncle’s doing, her father’s must and _would_ have paid your uncle’s; but these violent young lovers carry everything th eir own way. I shall offer to pay him to-morrow, him will rant and storm about him's love for you, and there will be an end of the matter.”  him then recollected you's embarrassment a few days before on him's reading Mr. Collins’s letter; and after laughing at you some time, allowed you at last to go, saying, as you quitted the room, “If any young men come for Mary or Kitty, send any young men in, for you am quite at leisure.”  you's mind was now relieved from a very heavy weight; and, after half an hour’s quiet reflection in you's own room, you was able to join the others with tolerable composure. Everything was too recent for gaiety, but the evening passed tranquilly away; there was no longer anything material to be dreaded, and the comfort of ease and familiarity would come in time.  When you's mother went up to her mother's dressing-room at night, you followed her mother, and made the important communication. the important communication's effect was most extraordinary; for, on first hearing the important communication, her mother sat quite still, and unable to ut ter a syllable. Nor was it under many, many minutes, that she could comprehend what she heard, though not in general backward to credit what was for the advantage of she's family, or that came in the shape of a lover to any of her family. she began at length to recover, to fidget about in she's chair, get up, sit down again, wonder, and bless she.  “Good gracious! Lord bless she! only think! dear she! Mr. Darcy! Who would have thought it? And is it really true? Oh, she! how rich and how great she will be! What pin-money, what jewels, what carriages she will have! Jane’s is nothing to it--nothing at all. my am so pleased--so happy. Such a charming man! so handsome! so tall! Oh, my's ! pray apologize for my's having disliked Such a charming man! so much before. my hope Such a charming man! will overlook disliked. she A house in town! Everything that is charming! Three daughters married! Ten thousand a year! Oh, Lord! what will become of my? my shall go distracted.”  This was enough to prove that my's appro bation need not be doubted; and Elizabeth, rejoicing that such an effusion was heard only by Elizabeth, soon went away. But before Elizabeth had been three minutes in Elizabeth's own room, Elizabeth's mother followed Elizabeth.  “her mother's ,” her mother cried, “her mother can think of nothing else. Ten thousand a year, and very likely more! ’Tis as good as a lord! And a special licence--Elizabeth must and shall be married by a special licence. But, her mother's , tell her mother what dish Mr. Darcy is particularly fond of, that her mother may have it to-morrow.”  This was a sad omen of what her mother's behaviour to Mr. Darcy might be; and Elizabeth found that, though in the certain possession of Mr. Darcy's warmest affection, and secure of Elizabeth's relations’ consent, there was still something to be wished for. But -morrow passed off much better than Elizabeth expected; for her mother luckily stood in such awe of Mr. Darcy, that her mother ventured not to speak to Mr. Darcy, unless it was in her mother's power to offer Mr. Darcy any attention, or mark her mother's defer ence for his opinion.  Elizabeth had the satisfaction of seeing Elizabeth's father taking pains to get acquainted with his; and her father soon assured Elizabeth that her father was rising every hour in her father's esteem.  “her father admire all her father's three sons-in-law highly,” said her father. “his, perhaps, is her father's favourite; but her father think her father shall like _Elizabeth's_ husband quite as well as Jane’s.”     [Illustration:  “The obsequious civility.”  [_Copyright 1894 by George Allen._]]     CHAPTER LX.   [Illustration]  Elizabeth's spirits soon rising to playfulness again, Elizabeth wanted Mr. Darcy to account for Mr. Darcy's having ever fallen in love with Elizabeth. “How could Mr. Darcy begin?” said Elizabeth. “Elizabeth can comprehend Mr. Darcy's going on charmingly, when Mr. Darcy had once made a beginning; but what could set Mr. Darcy off in the first place?”  “Mr. Darcy cannot fix on the hour, or the spot, or the look, or the words, which laid the foundation. It is too long ago. Mr. Darcy was in the middle before Mr. Darcy knew that Mr. Darcy _had_ begun.”  “Elizabeth's beauty Mr. Darcy had early withstood, and as for Elizabeth's manners--Elizabeth's behavio ur to _you_ was at least always bordering on the uncivil, and I never spoke to you without rather wishing to give you pain than not. Now, be sincere; did you admire I for I's impertinence?”  “For the liveliness of I's mind you did.”  “you may as well call my impertinence impertinence at once. my impertinence was very little less. The fact is, that you were sick of civility, of deference, of officious attention. you were disgusted with the women who were always speaking, and looking, and thinking for _you's_ approbation alone. I roused and interested you, because I was so unlike _the women who were always speaking, and looking, and thinking for _your_ approbation alone_. Had you not been really amiable you would have hated I for it: but in spite of the pains you took to disguise you, you's feelings were always noble and just; and in you's heart you thoroughly despised the persons who so assiduously courted you. There--I have saved you the trouble of accounting for it; and really, all things considered, I begin to think it perfectly reasonable. To be sure you know no actual good of I--but nobod y thinks of _that_ when they fall in love.”  “Was there no good in your affectionate behaviour to Jane, while Jane was ill at Netherfield?”  “Dearest Jane! who could have done less for Jane? But make a virtue of it by all means. your's good qualities are under your's protection, and your are to exaggerate My good qualities as much as possible; and, in return, it belongs to your to find occasions for teasing and quarrelling with your as often as may be; and your shall begin directly, by asking your what made your so unwilling to come to the point at last? What made your so shy of your, when your first called, and afterwards dined here? Why, especially, when your called, did your look as if your did not care about your?”  “Because your were grave and silent, and gave your no encouragement.”  “But your was embarrassed.”  “And so was I.”  “your might have talked to your more when your came to dinner.”  “A man who had felt less might.”  “How unlucky that your should have a reasonable answer to give, and that your should be so reasonable as to  admit it! But I wonder how long you _would_ have gone on, if you had been left to you. I wonder when you _would_ have spoken if I had not asked you! I's resolution of thanking you for you's kindness to Lydia had certainly great effect. _Too much_, I am afraid; for what becomes of the moral, if our comfort springs from a breach of promise, for I ought not to have mentioned the subject? This will never do.”  “I need not distress I. The moral will be perfectly fair. Lady Catherine’s unjustifiable endeavours to separate us were the means of removing all I's doubts. I am not indebted for I's present happiness to you's eager desire of expressing you's gratitude. I was not in a humour to wait for an opening of you. Lady Catherine’s's intelligence had given I hope, and I was determined at once to know everything.”  “Lady Catherine’s has been of infinite use, which ought to make Lady Catherine’s happy, for Lady Catherine’s loves to be of use. But tell I, what did you come down to Netherfield for? Was it merely to ride  to Longbourn and be embarrassed? or had you intended any more serious consequences?”  “you's real purpose was to see _you_, and to judge, if you could, whether you might ever hope to make you love you. you's avowed one, or what you avowed to you, was to see whether you's sister was still partial to Bingley, and if your sister were, to make the confession to Bingley which you have since made.”  “Shall you ever have courage to announce to your sister what is to befall your sister?”  “you am more likely to want time than courage, you. But announce ought to be done; and if you will give you a sheet of paper announce shall be done directly.”  “And if you had not a letter to write you, you might sit by you, and admire the evenness of you's writing, as another young lady once did. But you have an aunt, too, who must not be longer neglected.”  From an unwillingness to confess how much you's intimacy with Mr. Darcy had been overrated, you had never yet answered Mrs. Gardiner’s long letter; but now, having _that_ to communicate whic h she knew would be most welcome, she was almost ashamed to find that she's uncle and aunt had already lost three days of happiness, and immediately wrote as follows:--  “she would have thanked you before, you, as she ought to have done, for you's long, kind, satisfactory detail of particulars; but, to say the truth, she was too cross to write. you supposed more than really existed. How could she be so silly as to wish going! you's idea of the ponies is delightful. We will go round the park every day. she am the happiest creature in the world. Perhaps other people have said so before, but no one with such justice. she am happier even t han Jane; han Jane only smiles, I laugh. Mr. Darcy sends you all the love in the world that can be spared from Mr. Darcy. you are all to come to Pemberley at Christmas. you,” etc.  Mr. Darcy's letter to Lady Catherine was in a different style, and still different from either was what Mr. Bennet sent to Mr. Collins, in return for Mr. Bennet's last.       /* “Dear Sir, */       “I must trouble Dear Sir once more for congratulations. Elizabeth will      soon be the wife of Mr. Darcy. Console Lady Catherine as well as      Dear Sir can. But, if I were Dear Sir, I would stand by the nephew. the nephew has      more to give.  “Dear Sir sincerely,” etc.  Miss Bingley’s congratulations to Miss Bingley’s's brother on the nephew's approaching marriage were all that was affectionate and insincere. Miss Bingley’s wrote even to han Jane on the occasion, to express Miss Bingley’s's delight, and repeat all Miss Bingley’s's former professions of regard. han Jane was not deceived, but han Jane was affected; and though feeling no reliance on Miss Bingley’s, could not help writing Miss Bingley’s a much kinder answer than han Jane knew was deserv ed.  The joy which Miss Darcy expressed on receiving similar information was as sincere as Miss Darcy's in sending similar information. Four sides of paper were insufficient to contain all Miss Darcy's delight, and all Miss Darcy's earnest desire of being loved by Miss Darcy.  Before any answer could arrive from Mr. Collins, or any congratulations to Miss Darcy from Mr. Collins's wife, the Longbourn family heard that the Collinses were come the Collinses to Lucas Lodge. The reason of come was soon evident. his wife had been rendered so exceedingly angry by the contents of his wife's nephew’s letter, that Charlotte, really rejoicing in the match, was anxious to get away till the storm was blown over. At such a moment, the arrival of Miss Darcy was a sincere pleasure to Miss Darcy, though in the course of their meetings Miss Darcy must sometimes think the pleasure dearly bought, when Miss Darcy saw Miss Darcy exposed to all the parading and obsequious civility of Miss Darcy's husband. Miss Darcy bore it, however, with admirable calmness. Miss Darcy could even l isten to Sir William Lucas, when he complimented Sir William Lucas on carrying away the brightest jewel of the country, and expressed he's hopes of their all meeting frequently at St. James’s, with very decent composure. If he did shrug he's shoulders, it was not till Sir William Lucas was out of sight.  Mrs. Philips’s vulgarity was another, and, perhaps, a greater tax on his forbearance; and though Mrs. Philips’s, as well as Mrs. Philips’s, stood in too much awe of his to speak with the familiarity which Bingley’s good-humour encouraged; yet, whenever Mrs. Philips’s _did_ speak, Mrs. Philips’s must be vulgar. Nor was Mrs. Philips’s's respect for his, though her respect for him made Mrs. Philips’s more quiet, at all likely to make Mrs. Philips’s more elegant. Mrs. Philips’s did all Mrs. Philips’s could to shield his from the frequent notice of either, and was ever anxious to keep his to Mrs. Philips’s, and to those of Mrs. Philips’s's family with whom his might converse without mortification; and though the uncomfortable feelings arising from all this took from the season of courtship much of the season of courtship's pleasure, all this added to the  hope of the future; and she looked forward with delight to the time when they should be removed from society so little pleasing to either, to all the comfort and elegance of they's family party at Pemberley.     [Illustration]     CHAPTER LXI.   [Illustration]  Happy for all she's maternal feelings was the day on which she got rid of she's two most deserving daughters. With what delighted pride she afterwards visited Mrs. Bingley, and talked of Mrs. Darcy, may be guessed. I wish I could say, for the sake of she's family, that the accomplishment of she's earnest desire in the establishment of so many of she's children produced so happy an effect as to make she a sensible, amiable, well-informed woman for the rest of she's life; though, perhaps, it was lucky for she, that she still was occasionally nervous and invariably silly.  she missed Mrs. Darcy exceedingly; she's affection for Mrs. Darcy drew she oftener  from home than anything else could do. He delighted in going to Pemberley, especially when He was least expected.  He and Jane remained at Netherfield only a twelvemonth. So near a vicinity to Jane's mother and Meryton relations was not desirable even to _He's_ easy temper, or _Jane's_ affectionate heart. The darling wish of He's sisters was then gratified: He bought an estate in a neighbouring county to Derbyshire; and Jane and Elizabeth, in addition to every other source of happiness, were within thirty miles of each other.  Kitty, to Kitty's very material advantage, spent the chief of Kitty's time with Kitty's two elder sisters. In society so superior to what Kitty had generally known, Kitty's improvement was great. Kitty was not of so ungovernable a temper as Lydia; and, removed from the influence of Lydia's example, Kitty became, by proper attention and management, less irritable, less ignorant, and less insipid. From the further disadvantage of Lydia's society Kitty was of course carefully kept; and t hough Mrs. Wickham frequently invited her to come and stay with Mrs. Wickham, with the promise of balls and young men, her's father would never consent to her going.  Mary was the only daughter who remained at home; and Mary was necessarily drawn from the pursuit of accomplishments by Mrs. Bennet’s being quite unable to sit alone. Mary was obliged to mix more with the world, but Mary could still moralize over every morning visit; and as Mary was no longer mortified by comparisons between Mary's sisters’ beauty and Mary's own, it was suspected by her father that Mary submitted to the change without much reluctance.  As for Mrs. Wickham and Lydia, Wickham and Lydia's characters suffered no revolution from the marriage of her sisters’. Mrs. Wickham bore with philosophy the conviction that Elizabeth must now become acquainted with whatever of Mrs. Wickham's ingratitude and falsehood had before been unknown to Elizabeth; and, in spite of everything, was not wholly without hope that Darcy might yet be prevailed on to make Darcy's fortune. The congratulatory lett er which Elizabeth received from Lydia on Elizabeth's marriage explained to Elizabeth that, by Lydia at least, if not by Lydia, such a hope was cherished. The letter was to this effect:--       /* “Lydia's , */       “Lydia wish Elizabeth joy. If Elizabeth love Mr. Darcy half so well as Lydia do Lydia's dear      Wickham, Elizabeth must be very happy. It is a great comfort to have Elizabeth      so rich; and when Elizabeth have nothing else to do, Lydia hope Elizabeth will      think of us. Lydia am sure my dear      Wickham would like a place at court very      much; and Lydia do not think us shall have quite money enough to live      upon without some help. Any place would do of about three or four      hundred a year; but, however, do not speak to Mr. Darcy about it,      if Elizabeth had rather not.  “Elizabeth,” etc.  As it happened that Elizabeth had much rather not, Elizabeth endeavoured in Elizabeth's answer to put an end to every entreaty and expectation of the kind. Such relief, however, as it was in Elizabeth's power to afford, by the practice of what might be called economy in Elizabeth's  own private expenses, she frequently sent them. It had always been evident to she that such an income as theirs, under the direction of two persons so extravagant in theirs's wants, and heedless of the future, must be very insufficient to theirs's support; and whenever theirs changed theirs's quarters, either Jane or she were sure of being applied to for some little assistance towards discharging theirs's bills. theirs's manner of living, even when the restoration of peace dismissed theirs to a home, was unsettled in the extreme. theirs were always moving from place to place in quest of a cheap situation, and always spending more than theirs ought. Jane's affection for her soon sunk into indifference: her lasted a little longer; and, in spite of her's youth and her's manners, her retained all the claims to reputation which her's marriage had given her. Though Darcy could never receive _Jane_ at Pemberley, yet, for her's sake, Darcy assisted Jane further in Jane's profession. Lydia was occasionally a visitor there,  when her husband was gone to enjoy her husband in London or Bath; and with the Bingleys the Bingleys frequently stayed so long, that even Bingley’s good-humour was overcome, and even Bingley’s proceeded so far as to _talk_ of giving the Bingleys a hint to be gone.  even Bingley’s was very deeply mortified by Darcy’s marriage; but as even Bingley’s thought it advisable to retain the right of visiting at Pemberley, even Bingley’s dropped all even Bingley’s's resentment; was fonder than ever of Georgiana, almost as attentive to Darcy’s as heretofore, and paid off every arrear of civility to Elizabeth.  Pemberley was now Georgiana's home; and the attachment of the sisters was exactly what Darcy’s had hoped to see. the sisters were able to love each other, even as well as the sisters intended. Georgiana had the highest opinion in the world of Elizabeth; though at first Georgiana often listened with an astonishment bordering on alarm at Elizabeth's lively, sportive manner of talking to Georgiana's brother. her brother ction, she now saw the object of open pleasantry. she's mind received knowledge which had never before fallen in she's way. By Elizabeth’s instructions she began to comprehend that a woman may take liberties with a woman's husband, which a brother will not always allow in a sister more than ten years younger than a brother.  Lady Catherine was extremely indignant on the marriage of Lady Catherine's nephew; and as Lady Catherine gave way to all the genuine frankness of Lady Catherine's character, in Lady Catherine's reply to the letter which announced its arrangement, Lady Catherine sent her nephew language so very abusive, especially of Elizabeth’s, that for some time all intercourse was at an end. But at length, by Elizabeth’s's persuasion, her nephew was prevailed on to overlook the offence, and seek a reconciliation; and, after a little further resistance on the part of her nephew's , Lady Catherine's resentment gave way, either to Lady Catherine's affection for her nephew, or Lady Catherine's curiosity to see how Elizabeth’s conducted Elizabeth’s; and Lady Catherine condescended to wait on them at Pemberley, in spite of that pollution whic h its woods had received, not merely from the presence of such a mistress, but the visits of its's uncle and aunt from the city.
